RATING: 8.8
Excellent game. Thoroughly enjoy the theme, mechanics, and the evolving strategy of each gameplay. It forces you think think tactically and adapt intelligently on the fly. Playing with 3 players seems the most balanced. Two player games can feel a bit stacked sometimes -- the winner being determined early on -- and four players generally pushes the game into the "age 10" cards, which IMO sway the game more than necessary. However, this game is always fun to pick up, because the mix of cards ensures that each game plays differently than any game before it.

RATING: 8
This game is fantastic.

Of the four of us:
  Chloe loves it, the thinky, deep, organized chaos.  Lots of choices all the time that are meaningful and potentially game swinging.
  Maximus really likes the take-that nature of it.
  Molly thinks it's way too much going on and is immediately turned off by the chaos of play.
  Aaron thinks the game has an incredible balance and thinks the dogma of each card represents it well, the theme shines through and doesn't feel pasted on.

All in all, excellent fun for the thinky conflict oriented gamer.

RATING: 9
After getting Glory to Rome and realising I missed out on a great game for years because the graphics weren't that great, I decided not to let the same thing happen twice and picked up Innovation (asmadi version)

Innovation is a great game.  I can see why some people would not like it, but I think its amazing.

I'm 13 plays in at this point and I could easily see playing this game past 50 plays.  I see there are some people playing this past 100 plays and i'd believe it.

The game is chaotic fun, But i'll try explain what it is I love about it. 
There are 100 odd cards each with a unique ability.  The first game or 2 you'll feel overwhelmed and perhaps not in control of whats going on. but the more you play you get to know the cards.  You know that in each age there are 10 cards, 2 of the 5 colors.  So while it seems random you have a strong element of knowing what is possible, and the odds of getting a specific card, color or element, in any given age.

It's got simple rules, but it's an extremely deep game.

This plays best with 2, and good with 3. which works well with Glory to Rome being good with 2 and best with 3-5.

RATING: 10
The base set of Innovation is a great game.  With over 100 unique cards spread over 10 ages, you never have the same game twice.  You're always looking at what your opponent has, how they can effect you (and how you can thwart that), and how you can effect them.  It's a game with a high degree of interactivity between the players.

It is a game of synergies, like its cousin Glory to Rome.  Making the cards in your hand work together to score you points, tech you up, and increase your icon dominance via splays (all the while defending and attacking with card effects and icon superiority) provides an engaging experience, but not an overly complex one.  While there can be some chaos with more than 3 players, it's not often that you feel like your plans all just went out the window, though that can definitely happen; isn't that what screwage is all about?.

It's one of my very favorite games.  Lighter, more interactive, and shorter than Glory to Rome, Innovation has a lot to recommend it to the table.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
2013-05-10 (rating 8):

Somehow the game keeps dragging me in.

2011-09-23 (rating 6):

Far to random for me. No control over which cards are you getting. This is even worse when effect forces you to draw and mold a card - you don't know what will you draw and that could break a combo for example. Effects released by others also suffer from this as they cannot be target explicitly. Forcing your opponent to mold may make his position either better or worse. You don't know.

When playing with more players those drawbacks are even stronger.

To sum up an OK game that has nothing to offer for me.

RATING: 7.5
This is a quite complex game with simple rules. Everything is on the cards and every card is unique. The interplay with opponents makes the game shine - so it is best (for me) with 4 players. The flaws are around the swingy nature of the game - at times, one or two players can run away with the game, leaving others far behind. Some cards can be exceedingly unbalanced. But the game delivers a sweeping civ feel that is near absolute chaos and yet worth exploring. I like it alot!

RATING: 7
An excellent card game with lots of tactical decisions to make.  It does have a hectic pace that can put off some people, and because the board changes very quickly it may cause analysis paralysis in some players, but all in all it's a very good game.  As with any card game, there is some luck of the draw, but like any good card game, a skilled player can overcome a bad draw.  With more players, the game can become very swingy, so I'd recommend playing with fewer.

RATING: 6
I love this game two player. It doesn't scale well though IMO. At 4 players it begins to feel like the game resents you being involved. It runs like a petulant algorithm where you literally sit around while countless interdependent cards trigger actions amongst themselves as players take their turns until it is your turn again. It becomes impossible to plan ahead since everything is likely to change in front of you and everyone else by the time it gets around to your turn. My last 4 player game, I simply surrendered to the games will, waited until it was my turn, took some random action, and waited. I won. Still couldn't say why.

------
After remembering that multiple people on BGG mentioned this game along with Race for the Galaxy and 7 Wonders, I took a chance with out doing full research.

The 1st game took about 2 hours with 4 people.  However the game seems *way* too random due to over-powerful cards as you progress through the ages. I actually wonder if there is any real strategy with 4 people. In other words, I wonder what percentage of the time will a very good and experienced player win over a new player?  So far it looks like a coin toss.

RATING: 8
Innovation for the win. I am always surprised by people reactions to this masterpiece. It's too chaotic! It's not thematic! It's ugly! There's no strategy! I guess that living in a society with the attention span of a microbe doesn't help. Reality is this game wants you to innovate. Getting stuck with the same combo for 2 turns its a recipe for disaster. You have to constantly change ad adapt your strategy, embrace chaos and dig into those decks! You may come up with the right solution for your problems...a real Innovation! Can't give this one enough praise. Avoid with 4p, 3p only with people that know the game and like it!

RATING: 7
Another fantastic game from Carl Chudyk which hits me on all cylinders.  It's fast, chaotic, tactical, strategic (although you'll need to have some knowledge of the cards for that) and most importantly a lot of fun.  Features a lot of player interaction and tons of cards which work to perform various combinations.  As a Magic player I really appreciate the aspect of finding card synergy in this along with the overall variance in card abilities.  The components could be a bit better as the artwork and graphics are very plain, with the only positive being that the backside art is well laid out and appropriate (each age has its own font and style).  Innovation is definitely a more chaotic experience than Glory to Rome, but is much easier to explain and play from the get go with games usually clocking in under 45 minutes.  I prefer playing this with three players both to minimize downtime and for an overall 'easier play feeling' as two player games are too confrontational and require too much thinking for me.

RATING: 7
An interesting tactical game of combining special powers to build an efficient engine.  Appears to be rather chaotic and luck-driven, but I suspect that repeated play will minimize this.  Much like Race for the Galaxy can seem dominated by who draws the best hand, when in actual fact you can often do great things with a bad hand and a little luck.

My chief worry is with regard to the amount of direct player interaction (of the 'I steal your score cards' variety).  Not so much a problem in a two-player game, but it makes 3 and 4 player games a little wild.  Still, it's a short enough game to handle a little chaos.

RATING: 8.2
All of the Carl Chudyk games I've checked out are good, but Innovation just might be his best, a confrontational card-based civilization builder with a lot of those multi-purpose and multi-directional card mechanics his designs are known for. I am fortunate to have started in on the Iello edition of the game, which is an enormous graphic improvement over the original. Gameplay is wild, with a fair amount of decisions largely because of how you can use the cards, but it's not so crazy that it lacks an intuitive feel after a few rounds.

RATING: 10
Looking at my metrics I realized i haven't rated Carl's second masterpiece.

I will admit I hated this game the first time I played it. even being an early fanboy of GtR.  But... we played with four.  Don't do that.  or use teams if you must, but really, just don't.  Play it with three.  or two.  and then play it again.  It becomes a game (instead of an experience) once you know the cards.  Before that, it's a thrill ride type of game, and as a thrill ride game, it's just middle-of-the road.

But once you know what to expect from the piles, and once you know what certain cards will do to the game... then it's back in your control (within reason).

Brilliant.  Shouldn't be a long game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The best civilization game for me. It's got Chudyk's overloaded cards - they symbolize the technological level of your civilization, active advancements, score, achievements, and your ability to resist and prevent the sharing of card effects. Not to mention the number of ways those card effects interact with other cards!

I think any player count works well, here - as long as you do teams for 4 players. People say the game is chaotic, and they're right - but playing tactically amid the chaos is great. And better strategies that make the most out of what's available beat inferior ones.

I just don't like crapshoot cards like Alchemy and Physics - they mar the game space in my opinion. Cards that give you at least some benefit, like Metalworking, aren't as bad.

RATING: 7.3
Finding this extremely hard to rate, since the game is very swingy, and you can have quite a bad experience with the game if the luck of the draw just doesn't go your way. There are ways to mitigate this, but they themselves depend on card draws, and are non-obvious for inexperienced players. Despite that, I'm somewhat in awe of the innovation displayed in this design, as well as the progression through the ages, each with their own flavour and mechanics. 

So this rating is very much a "how much do I personally feel like playing it, compared to various other games"-rating. The game itself is a flawed but interesting breath of fresh air. You need to be able to shrug off bad experiences, and don't ever play it with 4, and then you might just enjoy it more than you'd expect.

RATING: 8
After the first few plays (with just 2 players: with more everything should be crazier...), this game is a tough beast to master: sometimes one can feel incredible depth, some other times it's frustrating when a huge swing of fortune mess up with your plans. This is definitely not a game for control freaks... Initial rating is 7.

Update june 14, 2012: After almost 20 plays, I am becoming addicted to the game! Innovation in my head is now part of a "holy trimurti" of great card games: Race for the Galaxy, Glory to Rome and, of course, Innovation.
When you begin to learn the cards, you can try to master the crazy wild horse and contain the huge swings.
Obviously the game is more strategic when played with just 2, while with 4 it can end abruptly with little to no control, but Innovation has in its DNA all the elements of a great game: constant tension (between USING cards and PLAYING cards), difficult decisions, easy rules, increasingly powerful unique card effects than lead to end-game climax... A wonderful, ingenious game by mr. Chudyk! Rating increased to 7.5.

Update april 25, 2015: while I can't stand a four player game, the three player is definitely good, and the two player is a wonderful, agonizing knife-fight in a roller coaster! Rating upgraded to 8 (and it would be a 9 if it was a 2-player only affair...).

RATING: 10
:d10-2:
Early experience with Innovation is dominated by individual cards. Since you don't yet know the full of range of possible actions, strong cards with non-obvious counters often go the distance. The game is now luck-based.

With more experience, you learn the counters to individual cards. How do you take out The Wheel and Metalworking? Engineering or Gunpowder. How do you take out Mathematics? Machinery or Societies. How do you take out Skyscrapers? Fission or just ending the game. Each card can be neutralized by one or more other cards, and thus the game becomes a scramble for the first "broken" card, and subsequently its counter or a "more broken" card. The game is now tactics-based.

With even more experience, you develop an awareness of game state patterns. You arrive at a certain game state and realize that you've seen it or something similar in a previous game, and you know its various branches. Your focus shifts to control of the game state, and you attempt to manipulate it to a position that will end in your victory. Good play sometimes entails passing up on achievements to establish board position, while other times it entails giving up board position to claim achievements. Either way, these actions are taken with a specific end in mind. It all depends upon the game state and how far ahead you can see. The game is now strategy-based.

In this way, it's a bit like jazz improvisation. Beginners play random notes that fit over the chord structure, with no sense of melodic logic except what feels right. Intermediate improvisers (such as myself) develop melodic patterns, or 'licks', that sound good at particular parts of the chord structure. They don't have a sense of the overall shape and scope of the improvisation, so they string together many of these melodic fragments into a pleasant-sounding, but ultimately meaningless, musical statement. Advanced improvisers have reached the point at which they cease to think about improvisation in terms of melodic patterns, and instead see it as a temporal canvas, where the architecture of the music is constructed at a macro level and descends from there.

RATING: 9
[i]Posted: 10/4/10, Rated: 9[/i]
{2P: 10, 3P: 9, 4P: ???}

My first game of [i]Innovation[/i] was an epic, 2p battle.  You can read the session report here: [thread=553768][/thread].  After that exhilarating experience, I had to get my own copy.  And as you might have surmised, I knew that I already loved this game.

[i]Innovation[/i] does exactly that.  The iconic "ring of cards" with the achievements in the middle challenges you from the get-go.  The seemingly-oversized reference cards soon develop their own personality, as you fan cards that have been scored to the left, and your achievements to the right.

Dominance in a "dogma" is determined by how many symbols appear on your played cards.  In a novel idea, cards start out in a stack, then get splayed to the left where a single symbol on each lower card is revealed, thus increasing your power.  In the middle game, cards can be splayed to the right instead, revealing two symbols on each lower card.  Finally, cards get splayed [i]up[/i], revealing three symbols on each lower card.  It's a very clever mechanism that imparts the idea of progress.

[person=6001]Carl Chudyk[/person] is also the developer of [i][thing=19857]Glory to Rome[/thing][/i], and there is a similar chaotic feeling to [i]Innovation.[/i]  I love civ builders, and was very surprised at how well a seemingly simple batch of cards embodies that genre.

RATING: 8
My feelings about this game are kind of mixed. I had a great first game. It was a two player game against my brother. I got some lucky first draws in the beginning, giving me what turned out to be a significant leap ahead. I also later had some very lucky timing moments for some very powerful dogma abilities. Needless to say, my brother fell behind very quickly. Based on my experiences playing games with my brother, I am usually not nearly as brilliant with my strategies and planning ahead as he is, choosing instead to rely too much on chaos and the luck of the draw, often only thinking one move ahead. Sometimes it works well enough to be close, and sometimes it fails me miserably. In this first game, I was so far ahead by the end, I felt there should have been some sort of mercy killing component in the rules. I had abilities to take all his points and abilities to take all his cards. My machine had effectively shut down all chances for my brother to even do anything.

That said, as we talked about it later, it was apparent be both saw some great potential in the game. It truly is well designed and worth the score I am giving it. The dogma abilities range from really cool to downright ruthless. And, honestly, I was impressed even when the ruthless card was being used against me. I suspect that my score for this game will only get better. And even though my brother had to experience being that far behind and so out of control, he also expressed a desire to play the game a lot more.

More nice things about this game? The artwork and overall card design is very well done. It's a tiny package. It doesn't take up too much space on the table. It sets up quickly. And if you already know the rules, it seems it would also be quick. I highly recommend it. And if you do get it and you have a bad game now and then with a runaway leader, I suggest simply giving the victory to him, reshuffling the cards, and starting a new game.

If this were easily available in Australia I'd buy it tomorrow.

RATING: 7
A very interesting card game!

Deeper than I thought. It requires strategy and some luck (card drafting) and it's quite a tense game. 
There's a lot of interaction, since most of the cards have a Supremacy Dogma or Cooperative Dogma. Either way, most of the cards will affect you, the good or the bad way.

Some cards, I guess, are overpowered and can win you the game. Some of them... from early ages, but still, is a game where the strategy takes its place. The mechanics are quite interesting, mostly the "splay" action, that makes the game shine.

I wouldn't play with 4 players, as there is too many things to focus on. With 2 is fantastic, with 3 still ok.

The game feels like an engine-game and you'll find it immersive. The problem here is... when you're really in the game, enjoying the way you splay your cards, and use their different abilities, someone rushes the endgame and puff... it's over, and you're still in age 6 ou 7.

I think the game could last a little bit longer, not that the game is quick (60min +/-), but you feel that it ends sooner that it was supposed.

Overall, a very pleasant game! Oh is it about innovations? Hmmm I guess so, but it's almost themeless!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Innovation is an incredible card game, well worth getting for almost any gamer, especially given the price.  The rules tell you what you can do, and how to win, but the mechanisms by which you score points (and become eligible to claim achievements, which is the goal of the game) are all special card powers.  This means that you begin your first game with no idea how you're actually going to be able to score points - this can be a bit frustrating if you're trying to learn the game from the rules (as opposed to being taught the game by an experienced player).  However, after a few turns, it will become clear.

The game has 110 cards, and every one is unique.  This means that the first few games have an element of discovery.  It makes the game move more slowly, but it is fun to draw a card with the feeling that it might do practically anything.  With more plays, players begin to know what they might find in any given stack, and play becomes more strategic, although I suspect this will always be a mostly tactical game.  

I have thoroughly enjoyed my plays so far, and look forward to a lot more.  This is a keeper, and a true gem.  I have only played 2er, which is fantastic, but suspect this would work just fine with a larger group.  For my wife and I, it is a great fit.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Chudyk is a genius. Mynew favourite designer.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Arrived: 7/29/2010
First play: 7/30/2010
Initial rating: 9

This is card based civilization game.  The first few plays will see players probably see whomever gets an early achievement lead taking the game.  This effect seems to dissipate after a few plays.

By play #4, my wife and I were always neck and neck on scoring achievements, with many being a 'if you hadn't gotten it just then, I was about to snag it'.  This was the case for the basic achievements as well as a tooth-and-nail fight for EMPIRE.

Scoring is critical.  If you cannot score you cannot achieve.

Splaying is important.  A  higher symbol count allows extra actions on other player's turns and prevent detrimental "I demand" effects.

Tucking is only really helpful if you've already splayed.

Drawing cards is helpful.  Teching ahead is more helpful but a built in 'catch up' mechanic means you won't keep a tech advantage for long if you don't maintain that pace.

In the end, this is a nice game that has a lot to explore.

RATING: 9
[b]Civilizations plus:[/b] One of the first contemporary games I bought, and still one of my favorites. I love the build-up from a stuttering two-technology prehistory through a digitized civilization (if you make it that far), and the quirky, yet somehow apt, interpretation of how technologies affect your tableau and abilities. I love that sometimes you have to break your civilization down in order to move it forward. And I love that it's highly interactive, sometimes crazily overpowered, and yet allows, maybe more than any other game I know, for exhilarating wins from behind. So much game in two decks' worth of cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My gaming group has really enjoyed this one. It's fast, exciting, strategic, and simple to play. It probably works best with 2 players, but we've found that the 3-player version works pretty well, too.

I must say that the theme definitely feels tacked on. Some cards reflect their subject matter brilliantly (e.g., Gunpowder allows you to blow up castles), but a lot of others will leave you scratching your head (why are Medicine and Vaccination attack cards?) That's a minor issue for me, though, since the gameplay is mostly good. 

My only issue with the gameplay is that things can get a little chaotic and random, especially toward the end of the game. Once the deck gets to the 9s and 10s, the game can end quite suddenly and unexpectedly, and it's often not the player who was dominating the whole game who emerges the victor. Sometimes this can be a positive feature, however, as even a player who had a miserable early game is never entirely hopeless, because they can try to get a 10 out and win that way. In 2-player games, however, the game will often end well before the late cards make an entrance.

The online version of this game (at innovation.isotropic.org) is completely free and works very well. It tends to play a lot faster than the in-person game, because the game will track score piles and icons for you.

RATING: 7.5
My first play of Innovation was four player, and yes, as you'd expect, it was no fun.  Thank god I trusted my instincts and picked it up anyway.  This is a great 2 player game - in fact, playing with more adds to brain-burn almost exponentially.  That said, the game is primarily tactical, so you can't feel too bad about losing.  Like Glory to Rome, the mechanisms in this one take a while to wrap your head around - particularly how to proceed when you don't have a card that gets stuff into your score stack.  Many have compared this to a juiced-up Fluxx, but it's much more fun than that.  Yes, it is quite dependent on the card draw, but like any good drafting game, there are more or less smart ways to take cards.  Our excitement for this one has cooled recently (at least, mine has, since I win all the time), and I'm pretty ambivalent about the idea of expansions.  That said, this is one of the most unique card games out there, it plays great with 2, and we enjoy it more than Glory to Rome.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
I have mixed feelings. I think the design is unique and really conveys the feeling of a Civ game in a short, all-card format. However, some of the cards are WAY too powerful, and can reward players who keep using the same powers over and over again, turn after turn, before others get a chance to resist them. 

The game is also a little too cruel/unforgiving for my taste. I often find myself either 1) really annoyed at what other players are doing to me or 2) feeling bad about how mean I'm being to everyone else. Resentment and guilt are two emotions I can do without on a Friday night with friends.

RATING: 6
I kept thinking "Fluxx" while playing this.  Add a few overpowered cards, a really random card selection where you can't choose to go in any particular direction, and a constant turn order problem concerning the VPs.. and you've got a clunker that may be able to be saved by revamping a few things.

Update:  I've played the finished game now and I've come around somewhat on it.  I don't think the game should be played with 4 at all, I think 3 is really the sweet spot for this, and I'm looking to try it with that amount.

2nd Update:  I played this with 2 and found it pretty enjoyable, though I'm starting to wonder if there's a runaway leader problem present.

3rd Update:  Yep, there's definitely a runaway leader problem in the game.  Also, this is not a 3 or 4 player game, you should only play it 2 player, and I think I'll try to only play it that way in the future.

---
The special powers are a little out-of-control here, and it's insanely chaotic, but I felt it was still pretty fun, in a "try to do the best you can given a constantly changing environment". Not to be played with anyone who suffers from analysis paralysis, as the analysis possibilities are insane. After one play I'm of somewhat mixed minds. Juggling the special powers is fun, but it may just be a bit too long, too involved, and too abstract for the chaos. Doesn't have the same charm as Glory to Rome, although that game wasn't without its flaws either. But still, I would enjoy playing again.

RATING: 8
Wow!  I was totally surprised at how much I liked this one!  I was a little worried about others' comments about how random the game was, but I thought it still managed to be very fun despite randomness of the draw.

The real fun here is figuring out how to make the best of the cards you get.  Sometimes you'll play a card because you want to use its dogma effect.  Sometimes you'll play it simply for its symbols to avoid being the victim of a "take that" card or to piggy-back on someone else's potential dogma effects.  Sometimes you'll play it in order to later score it.  Really, there are so many possibilities, which makes the ame a blast to play!

One word of warning... you're probably going to run into cards that seem way overpowered in your first game or two.  However, a card that is crazy awesome in one game has the potential to be nearly useless in the next.  For instance, gunpowder is awesome if players leave a bunch of castles on their stacks.  In our first game, this card pretty much single-handedly won the player that drew it the game, as the other two players were very castle heavy.  However, once players become familiar with the cards, they know this card is likely coming and try to plan accordingly.  As a consequence, gunpowder did not end up being game-breaking in the following game.  So, whether a card is balanced or not really depends on the players' familiarity with the cards and whether they're planning accordingly.  This may be a problem if you frequently teach the game to new players, or are not the type of group to play a single game frequently.

I also love tactical games just as much as strategic ones, and I would say that overall this game is highly tactical.  Yes, you need some strategic planning to plan for the appearance of certain cards in the game, but overall it's about capitalizing on the cards that you get, and cashing in on small windows of opportunity when they arise.

Overall, I had a great time exploring the cards and quickly jaunting through the inventions throughout history!  It's a very interesting and fun game, and I look forward to exploring it more!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6

[thing=63888][/thing] was my second purchase of the month, and I'm not sure what to think about it just yet (here’s a hint: I’m not optimistic).  Innovation is a medium-length card game from [person=6001]Carl Chudyk[/person], the designer of [thing=19857]Glory to Rome[/thing].  The comments about the designer employing outrageous swings of fortunate to balance each other are certainly accurate.  Unfortunately, each of the 100+ cards have unique (and often outrageous) effects, so putting the how, when, and why to initiate that balancing act in the hands of the players creates a costly entry barrier to the game.  Those who like the game tout its status as one that will take a lot of practice to master (and thus implying that it is a game with lots of replayability).  My worry is that it will similarly take a lot of practice to ever get proficient enough to make games strategic, tense, or interesting.

To give you just a sense of the complexity of the game, consider that each of Innovation's cards comes in one of five colors and contains three symbols, from any of six possible symbol types, located in three of four possible locations.  The way in which these symbols accumulate when played - which depends on how stacks of cards in each color are splayed (either stacked, splayed left, splayed right, or splayed up) - determines your civilization's strength in a number of areas.  Strength is important because each card contains a text-based power (or two) that may have repercussions for players with more or fewer symbols of the type displayed next to the power.  Confused yet?  I kinda was.  

My hope is that a little experience rounds the game into something similar to [thing=36218]Dominion[/thing]: a game in which every player can make reasonably good tactical choices every turn and potentially be in contention against players with roughly similar experience.  My hope is also that the game contains room to truly accommodate cutthroat strategic gaming with additional experience.  But it's really too early to know any of that.  At the moment, it feels a little closer to [thing=28143]Race for the Galaxy[/thing] [thing=258]Fluxx[/thing], where randomly drawn one-off card effects combine with a dense symbol-driven arms race to produce something both hard to control and hard to follow.  

The first play was little more than blindly stumbling through a morass of symbols that seemed quite overwhelming.  It also resulted in a runaway leader (me, to dispel any doubt that the leader was not also blindly stumbling), which is something that worries me at the introductory level.  The second play was closer, but only because my opponent put together a two-card combo halfway through the game that took all of my scoring cards and was based on symbols in which I was extremely deficient.  From there it was just a matter of turning the crank for enough rounds. 

Add to the complexity and the chaos an absence of blindingly obvious thematic elements and it seems that Innovation also has a lot of room to be a colossal flop for me.  My wife has already decided that she likes [thing=38797][/thing] better.  Considering I'm actively open to trading Uruk, that doesn't bode well.  Here’s hoping things turn around.

RATING: 10
This is a hard game to play properly. At first it felt like a wild advanced Fluxx ride, but after losing over and over again to better players, I realized that this is not Fluxx. 

You have a lot to pay attention to, icons, card effects, draw piles, scores, and you want to be ahead in both score and all icon counts, but that's unlikely to happen.

The tension in this game seems to come down to who's the player who's winning and who's the player who's losing. I know, that sounds like all games, but due to how players interact in this game, the winning player is often hammering one card over and over again in order to win, while a losing player is looking for a way to either neutralize that card (by icons, by stealing it, by covering it up) or finding a card that hammers better.

RATING: 8
Despite being a fan of tableau-building games, I resisted purchasing Innovation for a long time because the ideas and technologies in the game are incorporated in such a highly-abstracted form - moving cards around based on who has the most of a particular icon really did not seem that appealing.  One day, I tacked it onto a board game order and I am glad I did.  The actual mechanics of the game are very simple, but the interactions between the different dogmas give the game its depth.  The way splaying is used to convey progress is simple and elegant.  Furthermore, despite the abstraction, the dogmas do make sense thematically.  For example, "Gunpowder" destroys cards with Castle icons, allowing the attacker to score these.  This game is for those who enjoy exploring the interactions and combinations of card effects, and being a skilled player requires knowledge of the dogmas, which will only develop with repeated plays.  It is a very tactical game and can be somewhat chaotic at times.

RATING: 10

RATING: 5
Received as a generous gift from my good friends. I sleeved this game with MtG size sleeves (63 x 88 mm).

Initial rating an "8." I hear people refer to RftG, Dominion, etc. as "super fillers," and I didn't really have a game of that sort. Innovation is my addition for that niche, with some deep, fairly complex card interactions but relatively quick playing time.

What separates it from Dominion and RftG is that only Innovation scratches that "Magic the Gathering itch." In the prior options, card combos largely involve eeking out minor bonuses... but there's nothing game changing. With Innovation, however, certain cards are "rule breakers," with some odd swings and combinations. After two plays, we're only uncovering some of the possible synergies with cards. That's the MtG feeling... game-altering cards that allow you to bend the rules.

And yet, I grew tired of MtG because of the combative nature and the joyless approach that "serious" MtG fans took in the game. With Innovation, everyone shares in the same decks, and though there's direct conflict and interaction, there's also plenty of fun.

Update: two more plays, and this drops to a 6 and is trade bait. Yikes. That was one of the quickest plummets in my ratings. I think I reached that epiphany when I drew and "melded" a card, only to find out that I won the game through sheer dumb luck. I think it was one of the least satisfying "victories" I've experienced.

I really wanted to like this game, but the increasingly powerful abilities seem to cause the game to veer out of control. I think the other players felt the same way.

If thorough knowledge of the cards helps to create more tactical play, that's great. But I don't see this getting played frequently enough for me to reach that point. If I want "civ lite" in a card game, I'd rather play 7 Wonders. If I want to build a card tableau and activate card powers, I'd rather play London.

Update: traded it away for the Memoir '44 terrain pack. Ultimately, the "swingy" card effects outweighed all the other things I liked about the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
OK, I gave this another shot, and concluded that my previous rating of 2 was a bit ... churlish. I can at least see why some people like this game, even though I don't, and this isn't a pointless time sink in the way that other games I've rated at the two level. But I stand by my assertion that this is the most overrated game on the Geek at least in those games where the emphasis isn't Less Strategy, More Boba Fett.

My case against:

[i]Card draws are blind[/i]. It's almost impossible to target an overall strategy, because cards are whatever you draw off the deck. Compare London's card display, or Pax Porfiriana's card market, or Le Havre's set order of building proposals, or Fleet's license auction or Dominion's kingdom cards -- you have the ability to mentally draw an if-everything-goes-right strategic throughline right at the start, and minimize the degree that bad luck bites you in the butt. I'm not against games where it's hopeless to plan, but I tend to prefer those games to be short and punchy, and games of Innovation stop being fun for me long before they end.

The abstraction (I assume we have all accepted the fact that any pretensions on Innovation's part to being a "Civilization game" are laughable) of the game is a source of constant distraction. Insofar as I play abstract games (and, honestly, I usually don't) I prefer them to have a simple and elemental ruleset, and goverened as much as possible by raw skill.

Innovation is the game Double Fanucci was satirizing. (Oh, just Google it.)

I like plenty of games (see my Top 10) that are far more complex than Innovation, but grounding those games in theme makes all the difference. Innovation, though it has some good qualities, is ultimately the manipulation of signs without signifiers, leading to the arbitary crowning of someone as the winner. Some knowledge of the cards in the deck no doubt helps, as does instant recall of the many many ways to score "achievements" -- which are as distinguished from "points", for some reason -- but grinding away at a system and memorizing things is not the same as gaining skills.

Also: "dogma" is not a verb. Dammit.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Innovation is very close to what I consider a perfect game.  

1) It's easy to understand, you can learn all the basic mechanics in about 5 minutes or less.  
2) It offers a ton of replay value, since each time you play the game it will unfold in drastically different ways,
3) There is more than one victory condition available, and a variety of strategies to reach those conditions depending on what you encounter and what your opponents are up to
4) The game has a very nice feel of progression to it, as your civilization continuously becomes more powerful over time.  The technologies you acquire can become synergistic and represent more than the sum of their parts. 
5) It's a good balance between randomization and factors you have control over.  If you win, you may have gotten some lucky breaks but it was largely about your ability to take advantage of opportunities and make the right call.

The game is fairly balanced, but some technology combinations allow for runaway victories when finally put into place. A novice who is unaware of what they're doing can be completely dominated.  With players of similar skill, it's typical to have a close game.

I would consider this game a "must have" and I introduce it to almost everyone I ever play with.

RATING: 9
The key to the game seems to be to pick a horse, ride it until it collapses under you, and if you have done a god job of planning, you jump on the next fresh horse ready to go.

At the front end, the game will feel like pure, swingy, chaotic form, but upon further plays you will find cards to counter other cards and tactics to counter other tactics to make the game feel much more give and take.  This is one of those games that begs for exploration rather than taking it at first impression (though I will admit my first impression was overwhelmingly positive).

I will echo what others have said about the splaying mechanic feeling familiar and innovative all at the same time.

RATING: 8
Preliminary rating after 1 play. I won, but it seemed totally random. Turns are extremely tactical. Try to find something good for you that doesn't also help others. I really didn't like the special achievements. They seemed virtually impossible, without some special card which makes it significantly easier. But in that case, why not just put the goal on the card and scrap the general pool of special achievements? To play well, you'd really have to pay attention to what every other player can do. But it changes a LOT every turn, so it seems at least more fun to just play based on your own cards, but then it's pretty dang random. And I don't think I'm interested in really trying to track all other player's cards. And since you don't see any cards players might play before they actually take effect, it's still way random, even if you are trying to pay attention. Interesting system though.
---
Update: I hear it's better with 3 than 4. I would be willing to it again with 3.
---
Update: this game stuck in the back of my mind for some reason, and I eventually picked it up. Partially because my family really liked Glory To Rome, but I grew tired of it. I tend to not like games with crazy cards that do crazy thing, but I'm finding that my family [i]does[/i] like these kinds of games, and I'm happy playing what the family likes. But part of the reason I tend to not like games with cards that do crazy things is that you need to play a bunch of times to know what all those cards are and do. But since my family tends to like to play the same game over and over (not so much my preference, but again, playing with family is great, so I go along), games like this do get better with repeated play. I still find it slightly tedious to have to pay attention to every card that every player has in front of them, but I see us moving toward a point where we have all the cards memorized, and will know what they do from title alone. The game is probably best for people who plan to play enough to get to that point.
-----
Update: it's now a favorite of the wife & son. Replaced Glory To Rome as our 3p card game of choice. A couple of expansions into it now. Still a lot of longevity left I think.

RATING: 8
In keeping with Carl Chudyks apparent design paradigm "Make everything broken and escalate from there", Innovation is a highly fluent (at times volatile) game of engine building where players need to switch from offense to defense in the blink of an eye. Players need to be able to react to opponent's moves while fishing for powerful card combinations themselves. Knowing the deck is essential to keep the emerging chaos in check. But is it fun? Yes, loads of!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Permanent place in my top 5 favorite games for the past few years. This game plays uniquely every time with wonderful player interaction, shifting strategies and avenues of victory, and nearly endless possible combinations between cards that make the flow of the game fascinatingly unpredictable. The symbolic iconography and elegant mechanics with the five colors and multi-directional splaying add to the appeal of the game immensely. I love how the dogma effects reflect civilizations sharing or dominating technological and social developments on the basis of accumulated icons which represent the state of progress in various spheres of the player's civilization (civic, religious, military, economic, etc.).

One of the greatest things about this game is the relatively short time it takes to play - usually about 45 minutes to an hour, even with 4 players. We usually pull this one out when we only have a little time or need a filler, but it's always one we look forward to, and always satisfying - even when nuclear weapons come out and blow the world back to the dark ages with fission, or a player who was previously in last place benefits from the sudden emergence of environmentalism or the rise of the machines when A.I. and software come out. That part of the game adds an arbitrary element that makes it even that much more exciting and pretty funny when the loser suddenly becomes the winner, after the clear winner fails to secure a victory with all of his resources.

Deceptively simple in design, yet quite complex in its possibilities, Innovation provides an adequate challenge for any level of player, and lots of fun every time.

RATING: 8.5
This is one of those games that pretty much demands repeated plays to get underneath it.  There are myriad possibilities and getting to know the cards is a clear advantage.

Players are each turn choosing to play cards, draw them, or use the abilities of those already played.  The goal is to gain enough cards in your score pile.  The one key thing to realize (which took us a bit too long to figure out) is most of this can happen by stealing cards from other players.

REVISED Feb 2014: Once we connected that this is ideal as a 2-player game, we liked it immensely and I think it's going to be a go-to for us.  Lots of great strategies to unearth.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2.5
This game is primarily a tactical one where you don’t plan that much ahead strategically. The game’s own manual confirms that it is supposed to be like that, it says: "While long term planning can be done, it is often much more important to seize the best tactical option at any given moment."
However, the game has *a lot* of options. Particularly, it has 105 different cards, most of which have a long and often rather complicated text. After the opening of the game, most of the time each player has five different cards layed out in front of him that he could use (with a dogma action). Additionally, he has cards on his hand that he could play and use with a dogma action on his turn. Which means you have to read a lot of cards each turn which takes *a lot* of time. If you want to orient yourself and make a good play also considering your opponents possibilities to react, that is. Next turn, many top cards will likely have been replaced by other cards, so you’ll have to do much reading again, particularly with more than 2 players.
I suppose if you play the game a lot, at the time you know all 105 different cards and the ages they come in by heart, it’ll play more fluently. But 105 different cards, most of them with complicated effects – that takes a lot of games or memorising time! I *might* be willing to invest this time if it were a deep strategic game.
But the effects of the cards seem much too chaotic and unpredictably game changing for considerable long term strategic planning most of the time. (Yes, I realize that some planning is certainly possible.)
The cheap design with its ugly or rather practically non-existent art on the cards is a further turn-off.

Bottom line: Much too chaotic to be a deep strategic game, much too time-consuming with lots of complicated text and effects to be a light tactical game. Which results in the game completely failing in both categories as far as I am concerned.

I have a hard time explaining the positive ratings and comments here. I can only imagine that they are mostly by players which are OK with playing a game in "wing-it-mode" and have no desire to play anywhere near optimally, at least until they have played so much that they know all cards by heart. If you simply do something which seems nice at first glance and don’t even bother reading most of the layed out cards, it doesn’t take so long. If you like to play like that, Innovation might be for you. Otherwise, my advice is to steer well clear of it.

RATING: 9
I'm very impressed with how this game allows strong board-position leaders to be toppled from their throne, yet counts that transient dominance towards winning (via achievements). 

I'm less fond of four-player games, partly due to the greater chaos between turns (though this can be mitigated with team play), but more due to the fact that card stacks run out so quickly that "teching up" via dogma effects becomes very weak.

Players seem to first get better at this game by learning basic principles ("more icons is good"), learning what cards/powers exist, and being less overwhelmed and so better able to spot advantageous combos. I have to figure out how much advanced skill is simply more of the preceding vs. deeper understanding of gameplay.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I want to play this enough that we choose to go ahead and allow our opponent to do "our action" because we realize it will benefit us more or perhaps even solely if they should also do it. We don't do that enough so far I don't think.

RATING: 6
Components: Lots wrong with these cards.
1. The subdued card colors are difficult to distinguish by their primary color names (e.g. “red” is actually a faint pink).
2. The icons are too large, and the text too small.
3. The unique icon for the card is unnecessary and clutters up the card.
4. The dominant icon (used to activate the dogma) should be larger; it's difficult to see those of my opponent.
5. The icons should have names; we had to make up our own interpretations just so we could talk about them.
6. Lots of confusing wording on the cards.

Experience: I had thought I'd read about this game as being in the same family as Race for the Galaxy, Glory to Rome, and other games where the luck of the draw is balanced out by a growing tableau of strategic options. But Innovation doesn't share in the spirit of these games; it is a chaotic, ruthless take-that affair, where no card is safe.

I enjoy several of the clever mechanisms, including how using my own non-demand dogma can cause the opponent to do something they don't want to do. But it's also possible that using my own dogma will hurt me more than help me.

If I became more familiar with the cards, perhaps I would learn how to better defend myself. But in my three playings, my opponents and I could barely take a turn without some card moving from one side to the other, or a score card being stolen, or burning off a top card. Vicious, vicious, vicious. Even more chaotic with three than with two.

First Played: 2011-08-21
Last Played: 2013-02-26
Eagerness to Play Again: Within a year

Plays (3):
2 players: 2
3 players: 1

RATING: 9
It's great fun with two players, and I feel the mix of luck and strategy is just about perfect. Every turn the conditions change and you have to change with them if you want to win. Finding the right way to combine your cards to exploit your advantages (and your opponent's weaknesses) is the key, and the cards are well designed enough that there's *nearly* always something you can do, even when the situation looks bad. Four+ player games and three hour games are all well and good, but I don't get many opportunities to actually play them. This I can play with my wife for an hour after dinner, and that's just what I was looking for.

RATING: 4
There's some new and interesting stuff going on here: the splaying vs stacking; the dependency on your strength for the ability to leach off actions; the progression through the cards/powers building to big plays; the use of cards for a multitude of things.
But there are so many cards with so much text that its very hard to keep track of all the consequences of an action. There's a lot of leaning over the table to read & reread whats in front of every player. And that really bogs the game down and makes it slow and tiresome. Yet if you don't do that, the game becomes largely random where a win will come from someone not noticing how much benefit they're about to give you.
This kind of complaint can be levelled at many games and I usually dont have a problem (I love a lot of other text-heavy card games). Often its just a matter of playing the game a few times until everyone remembers and recognises the cards easily. But this one was too far for me. I cant see playing it enough to reach that point. Too much text for too little game play off for me.

RATING: 10
Rating after a single play, may go up. I'm biased toward quick cardgames with a small footprint (Morels, Jaipur, Citadels, Sleuth) and this didn't disappoint.

Edit: After a half dozen plays I'm still not sure about this one. Clearly you have to have a lot of familiarity with the cards to get the most out of it. It's always enjoyable, but there's a sense that the game is playing you. Not because there aren't meaningful choices, just that the amount of text on each card, and the number of cards in play, can be totally overwhelming. 

Edit: After a half dozen more plays, this game is awesome. Granted, sometimes it's a landslide for one player and there's nothing the other can do, but it doesn't seem to matter. From a design perspective, I have no idea how a card can be totally broken in one game and totally useless in the next. Innovation is really about recognizing which card is good in the current game and forgetting about the last one. If you love tactics, this is as tactical as a game can get.

Edit: The game continues to improve. After some twenty plays I finally introduced an expansion, Figures in the Sand. I love it. This is the best card game ever, no hyperbole. 

Regarding my prior comment that one player can run away early, I've also seen more completely unexpected comebacks in Innovation than any other game. If you fall behind you should just think, "Oh, good, I get a chance to do something epic," and fight on.

I still can't raise my rating to a 10 because it is impossible for many players to enjoy, or for me to enjoy with AP prone players, like my gf. If people aren't willing to just 'feel it and go', even if that means not grinding out the optimal play every turn, this can be a torturous experience. It's definitely a gamer's game only.

EDIT: Innovation continues to evolve approaching thirty plays, and continues to share no common ground with anything else I've played. Brilliant and entirely unique.

EDIT: Another amazing session tonight. Nuclear war wiped the board one action ahead of my win. Then I came back and lost one action behind. Increased my rating to a 10. No game works with every group. With the right people, Innovation is perfection.

RATING: 5
This is a self contained card game about playing card combos (like Magic).  It is quite complicated to learn and tedious to play for the first 5+ times until you get to know some of the cards by heart.  Part of the problem is that the game is not very thematic (which was the reason I was first attracted to the game).  Another part of the problem is that the names used for the actions are weird (for instance, playing a card is called "Melding").  But the biggest problem is that new cards get played face-up on every turn, and these cards change the options available for play.  So while you're reading your cards and trying to figure out what they do ("Meld", "Splay", "Tuck", "Achieve", etc) with text that is sometimes poorly worded, other people are playing cards which affect you (and which you can sometime use).  The end result is that the biggest aspect of the strategy is to simply keep up with all of the options that are on the table.  This is a recipe for Analysis Paralysis, so make sure you don't play this game with someone who has to review every option before making a decision.  But even without AP prone players, it feels like work to try and keep up with all of the changes going on.  These changes are sometimes not subtle at all.  In fact, many of the cards that get played are game changing.

This leads to one aspect of the game which is interestingly balanced.  The cards that get played are increasingly powerful and chaotic, so as the game progresses, things can change quickly.  Although it may seem like one player has the game locked up, a card may come up that turns everything on it's head.  I normally think chaos is a bad thing, but in this case, it is a type of controlled chaos that makes the game feel very dynamic.  Just don't expect that dynamic feel to be connected to a theme, because it is not.  Sometimes your civilization moves forward, sometimes backwards, and always in a manner that makes no realistic sense.

So if you are looking for an abstract card game with tons of options (to the point of overwhelming), then give Innovation a try.  I can see why people like this game.  There is something very interesting to the abstract mechanics that lead to card combos.    But when it comes to abstract card games, I have to recommend Dominion over Innovation.  Dominion is simpler, and very likely has more divergent and interesting strategies (though I can't be sure until I play Innovation more).

RATING: 8
Innovation is an exciting card game design by Carl Chudyk that I think is ultimately superior to his other well known design, Glory to Rome. This is not because I think that Glory to Rome is bad, I rate it a 7 and I think it is an enjoyable and solid design. I think that due to its similarities to Race For the Galaxy I can’t help but compare them and ultimately find Glory to Rome to be inferior. I understand that others disagree, and can appreciate it. There are some definite things to like about Glory to Rome (particularly some of the organic zaniness that the game), but I think the variability and depth of play that Race For the Galaxy provides pushes it to levels that Glory to Rome just cannot compare to. 

So what Innovation does is take some of the crazy abilities and organic feel of Glory to Rome and put it is in its own, easier to understand, game. The rules are far, far simpler than those you find in either Race For the Galaxy or Glory to Rome. There are four easy to understand basic actions and a symbol-set that is much easier to decipher than that of Race For the Galaxy or Glory to Rome. The only thing that really gets in the way of the game’s decipherability is the fact that each card has a different set of abilities and thus new text to absorb. This results in the game feeling more chaotic than it actually is, as it takes quite a few plays to absorb all of the options and paths the game has to offer, and get to the strategic and tactical meat that the game offers. At twenty five plays I only feel like I barely have a grasp on what is going on, and I am only now getting an idea of what some of the later cards do. Other, more studious, players will probably get to this point earlier, but regardless of how long it takes it is a lot of information to absorb. 

This constant tension between being forced to decide if you want to play cards or use powers that will help you with your current situation or the current board state and making decisions to build your infrastructure for the longer game is one of the main things that I like about the game. The cards have a nice mix between cards that will allow you to splay (reveal more symbols) and tuck (place cards at the bottom of your stacks and thus potentially give your more symbols) and gain small amounts of points or just put a new card on your board. And while each of these options also includes a bit of the other (short term gains will frequently help you long term and vice versa), figuring out which combination of long term or short term is ultimately better is fun and challenging.

While there are definitely cards that are better than others in the early game and the ability to gain a definite advantage over your opponents, the fact that the majority symbol of the early game (castles) goes away and other symbols (factories and clocks) appear later also allows players who are in a weak position early to come back for a win, alleviating luck and allowing players to come from behind even if they don’t get any strong early cards. It also creates an incentive for players to focus on either strategies that result in a shorter game (victory point/achievement rush) or longer game strategies (points or one of the unique victory conditions from the 9 and 10 decks). 

I think Innovation is a worthy addition to the highly replayable ability-based card game family. It is different enough from Race For the Galaxy/Glory to Rome, Dominion and the board game knock offs that it is a worthwhile addition to the collection of anyone who likes these games. Fans of Carl Chudyk’s works should be especially impressed. I rate it an 8, and expect that even as its plays climb that it will remain within 1 point of this value. Whether it goes to 9 depends on whether it continues to capture my attention and interest (like Race For the Galaxy) or fades away as its flaws become greater than its strengths (like Dominion or, to a lesser extent, Glory to Rome).

RATING: 3
Civilization based card game where players are trying to win the game, mainly through acquiring achievements.  Mechanically simple game where a player takes two actions for their turn.  Actions can be draw cards, meld cards, acquire achievements or enforce their dogma.  Draw and meld are sort of obvious.  Dogma is where things get interesting.... they action one of their melded cards, semi-forcing their opponents to toe the line.  The main objective is to get cards into their score pile, which is used to pick up achievements, which are required to win the game (usually).  Fun game, but some niggles - I felt like there was about 10% control and 90% ride in the game.  Definitely needs some more plays to refine the opinion - not sure it will get them though.  Repeat plays have confirmed - you can't take this one too seriously, it's a chaotic mess.

RATING: 6
A fairly involved card game that represents the flow of technology across a large historical timeline.   Each card has different powers, so the learning curve is a little high, sort of like Race For The Galaxy.   There is a lot of direct conflict in the game, so it gets a reputation for being chaotic, but I think its more volatile than chaotic.   Your scoring pile is basically defenseless, and you need to claim achievements when it is over the current high water mark.   It takes some effort to find the right cards that will enable you to score (at all) and that can be frustrating.   Graphically, it's a very ugly game, which is a shame, because it could have a lot more appeal if only it looked better.   Might not be a short game with AP-prone players, although I know it can move quickly too.  (P.s. the iello version in 2013 solves the ugly problem).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Innovation is an interesting game where ones board situation (score/abilities) is constantly changing.  This is a highly tactical and interactive game.  More so than a lot of the other 'high profile' card games that have been released withing the past few years.  When you start the game you have one power which will have 3 icons.  From there you get 2 action points on your turn which can be used to draw, lay a card to your tableau (this costs no cards just 1 action), get a victory condition (you need 6 to win) if you have the proper amount of score value, or use a special power.  You are only able to have 5 varieties of cards on your tableau, so if you play a blue card, that card will be placed over the other blue card on the table unless you have no blue cards and then you just start a blue pile.

So what do I think of the game?  Well, it's highly chaotic and the game feels very fragile with very little control in the hands of the players.  I won't say that this is Fluxx and the game plays you, but I feel that the way the cards come out and when certain cards are introduced can result in huge swings in the game.  It's very hard to prepare for cards even ones that you know are coming up based on the luck of the draw.  That being said, the game is pretty good and it's certainly different from everything else out there.  I think it's more fun just to see the way games can unfold more than having a high motivation on winning the game.  I think the game is best played with 2.

Beyond the game feeling a little fragile, I should also caution against the fiddly set-up/breakdown of the game.  In addition to that the game usually takes up a fairly large footprint on your table based on the fact that there are 10 decks of cards and once you start splaying mass cards, or have 15+ cards in your score pile, it becomes a little unwieldy.

RATING: 10
I find it fascinating how well this game brings the idea of inter-relatedness and context into focus.  It’s not that “x” or “y” card in and of itself is overpowered or game breaking.  It’s true that, at times, so many of them can be – but it’s also true that it is completely dependent upon the context in which the card finds itself in relation to the rest of your [i]and[/i] your opponent’s tableaus.  What nets you a win in one game can be the cement shoes that sink you in the next. 

It’s what makes the game feel so chaotic and even random at times.  It’s also what tempts you to believe that this is a luck-based game.  But I don’t think that's true.  I think that what the game does is challenge you to be able read and understand that related-ness, that context, and then act or react accordingly.  

Bluntly, even with a simple rule-set, it’s an enormous challenge as no two cards are the same.  Still, I'll say this, if you don’t immediately reject Innovation as “not my style of game,” then get ready because the epiphany train is getting ready to roll as there are endless layers and nuances to discover.  It's quite the masterpiece.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
[1 play] This was not at all what I expected. I was expecting something really unique and interesting.  What I got was a somewhat Fluxxy card game with little to no civilization-building feel to it.  

One thing I’d heard on BGG that made me very wary was complaints about the cards being difficult to see.  I don’t have the keenest sight, and have had some potentially good gaming experiences pretty much ruined for me by sitting where I could not see essential game elements.  But when I saw how this game is, it wasn’t such an issue for me.  There are more than 100 unique cards, each with its own special effects.  As the game goes on, each player builds up a tableau of cards in front of them. Ideally, you would want to know what cards the other players have down, since they can implement the powers of any of those cards on their turn, and you could be affected.  But I just decided early on that this was a learning game, and I was going to be occupied paying attention to all the cards in my own tableau and hand, and I was OK with not knowing what the other players were going to do until they did it.  It would indeed be annoyingly difficult to be constantly trying to read everyone else’s cards upside down and remember what they all are, especially as some of the color backgrounds are not the easiest to read upside down at any distance.  

I think that where this game would most shine would be if a group played it enough to get to know all the cards and their functions.  Then you’d only need to be able to read the card titles, which are in large bold print, or it’d be more practical to announce those as they were played.  If you knew the game well enough, you’d just need to know/see/hear that your opponent was playing Rocketry, or Writing, or whatever, and you’d know what that did.  I think the game could be enjoyed playing on a really casual level, as I did, and not bothering about what all the card effects were, or on a serious, much more informed level, knowing all the cards.  But in between, I can see how people could find it very strategically frustrating because of how time-consuming it would be to decipher all the information that could affect your plans. 

The game had a Fluxxy feel to us because many cards allowed people to do really sweeping things, including destroying huge chunks of what others had accomplished.  You progress toward victory sort of in two ways: first, you have a scoring pile to which you add cards with values between 1-10.  Second, you use your score pile to capture achievement cards, of which there are 10 (one for each of the 10 ages in the game).  There are also 5 special achievement cards, sort of wonder of the world type things, which can be obtained by meeting unique special conditions.  You win by getting 5 of these 15 achievement cards (in a 3p game, which is what we had).  

So in our game, one person was clearly ahead the whole time on the scoring thing.  When the second player finally got on the board with some score, the first one did some sweeping move that allowed him to steal it all away from her.  Meanwhile, I was focusing on the special achievement cards, and managed to set up the special conditions to bag four of them.  This was a successful tactic because mostly nobody realized what I was doing till I did it, and once you have achievements nobody can take them away from you (unlike your score pile, which can be attacked in various ways).  I was then able to win by getting a regular achievement for my 5th one, which took only about 2 turns because I had a card that let me put my whole hand (and I had a lot of cards in my hand) into the score pile.  

The notion of the ten ages was clever, but the theme didn’t feel strong.  And I think the drastic shifts helped undermine any kind of civilization-building feel, because this game very much didn’t feel like you were successfully building anything up over time.  It was more a bunch of wild random smitings where anything anyone did could suddenly be completely destroyed.  

If we played this game again, we’d all have a better idea of how to maintain some strategic balance of power.  And the game might rise to a higher level if you took the trouble to learn all the cards.  But it didn’t appeal to me enough for me to pursue it.

RATING: 8.5
By Carl Chudyk, designer of Glory to Rome, and features similar tactical fun.  A civilization-themed game with 105 unique technology cards, each with special effects that players use to earn points and achievements.  Keep an eye on this one folks, it could be a hit!

It's more fragile and luck-driven than Glory to Rome and harder to develop long-term strategies because it's more tactical, somewhat chaotic at times, and card draw can be important. But the combos and plays are so much fun, and if that's what people like about GtR, they'll find some of that here too. It does take a few plays to get familiar with the cards and technologies, but the basic rules are very easy, despite having some innovative mechanics.
I recommend this game very highly - if you're looking for a solid game that features strong card management, and where every card has a unique ability, and where you're going to have a ton of fun using your technologies together in innovative ways, then Innovation is for you.  

See my comprehensive pictorial review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/528778

Errata for specific cards: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/565605

RATING: 9
Played 3 times.  This game is crazy fun!

Edit 5/26/10.  I've now played a bunch of times:  7 x 2player, 3 x 3player, 1 x 4player.  This remains a very fun game, but it definitely has the potential to bog down if people feel the need to thoroughly consider their options.  4 player, especially, felt like it ran a bit long, though admittedly I was the only one who'd played before.  It seems that until all players have played enough to know the ins and outs of the cards, it's a lot more enjoyable if people don't think too much, which may naturally turn some gamers off.  But the mechanics are so clever and the game is so wildly addictive that this is a must-try for anyone who can deal with randomness and chaos.  I'm keeping this at a 9 because, at this point, this is a game I would never turn down.

RATING: 9
Having played Innovation (the beautiful [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/image/1440811/innovation]English 2nd Edition[/url]) for the fourth time, I can safely rank it at a solid 9. It is a pocket civilization game, full of discovery and a sense of increasing empowerment. Card effects frequently feel broken and unfair at first sight, and players who are expecting eurogame-style balance may get offended. But there are always counterplays and defenses available for the creative player. The game can be learned in five minutes and the design is elegant and very satisfactory, with the possible exception of the five "domains" cards, which I find an unnecessary complication. All in all, right now I enjoy it more than the famous [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/19857/glory-rome]Glory to Rome[/url] by the same Carl Chudyk, also an excellent, and probably superior, game.

RATING: 10
[b]One of my two favorite games. So much variety from game to game, and such a fun challenge. The 3 player game is fun, but fairly random; as a 2 player game, this is just stellar.[/b]

Rating history: 6|8|8|10|10|10|10|10|10.......|9.|10

1st play: 6. Apr/14, 4p. A hash - we thought you could splay cards from the very beginning (and thus saw no value in any cards that allowed you to splay). No one had any fun, and it felt like there was no control (4 players). I imagined the game WITH the correct rules, and still wasn't feeling it. Apr 2014.

2nd play: 8. Apr/14, 2p. The next night WITH correct rules - and wouldn't you know it, I had a lot of fun. I see what people mean when they say that you need to play the game a lot to understand the possibilities (which will give more of a feeling of control), but even it's my newbie chaos phase, I find it fun to play as a tactical game. Apr 2014.

3rd play: 8. Jul 2014, 2p. Hadn't played in a while and it took a bit to get the hang of it again. At least I finally won!

4th play: 10. Aug/14, 2p. WOW! This game finally clicked! I *LOST* 6 achievements to ZERO, and am still bumping this up to a 10!

5th play: 10. Aug/14, 2p. Taught it and lost again, still a 10.
6th play: 10. Aug/14, 2p. Taught it again...and lost again.
7th play: 10. Aug/14, 3p - Taught it again, and my first 3p game. The game is FAST with 3p, but still fun. More "I demand..." opportunities.
8th play: 10. Aug/14, 2p.
9th play: 10. Aug/14, 2p. Best playing of this so far. Down 0-4, then tied 5-5, I won 6-5 with a Special Achv. TONS of scored cards, very little raiding.
10th: 10. 3p game. Fission to zero out a splay leader. Alt condition for win! 08/14
11th: 10. Solitaire, using http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/573834/solitaire-mode-it-was-inevitable . Very challenging!
12th: 10. Solitaire. Self-Service for the win.
13th: 10. Solitaire. AI genetics into Robotics (with AI and Software on the board!) and 29 points. I was at 30. Next turn win. Phew!
14th: 10. 2p - Behind 2-4, I raided D's score pile with Vaccination, then Empiricism alt win (20 bulbs).
15th: 10. 2p - Early splays plus lucky Optics gave me a quick lead I rode to victory.
16th: 10. 3p - Tucked 8 to score 8, giving me two Specials for the win.
17th: 10. 2p - I scored early and often, getting achievements 1-4 very quickly. Then I Evolutioned into some 8's to finish the game 6-0.
18th: 9. 2p - It finally fell a notch. It was a 10 while every game played out differently, but now some gzmes have similar "feel". Still, an incredibly fun, tactical game.

20 plays in: 10. One of my two favorite games. So much variety from game to game, and such a fun challenge. The 3 player game is fun, but fairly random; as a 2 player game, this is just stellar.

RATING: 8
[2 plays] I don't know how much faith I have in this becoming one of my absolute favorites, but just in terms of game design, the last time I saw a game so obviously brilliant was Napoleon's Triumph.  

The civ aspect, although completely abstract, came through a lot more than I thought it would if sometimes seeming almost too fluid.  I really felt like our civilizations were clashing on many different levels and leveraging the relative strengths to maximum benefit. It's certainly interesting to see an attempt at anything of the sort in such a short period of time.

Having only played Glory to Rome, I already sense a clear Carl Chudyk signature in this game.  Though not mechanically similar in any way, fans of GtR would do well to consider this one without playing first.  Like GtR, all of the cards seem utterly broken at one point or another, but work together to make this completely insane, unpredictable whole.

RATING: 1
I don't mind the pasted on "theme" or the unnatracitve cards but this is a a poor excuse for a strategy game.  It rewards memorizing the deck more than tactics, planning or strategy.  Your first play will feel essentially like fluxx, but your friends will convince you that no; it's actually a deep great game that puts Race for the galaxy and mottainai to shame.  So you try again.  Halfway through you get tired of reading 10 different cards each turn to figure out what exactly is going on so you just start guessing at moves ti you're put out of your misery so you can play something else.  Trusting your friend's analysis you'll play again and again but the promised payoff never comes.  Even when you learn the cards, it is a load of "I hope he doesn't have that one card!"  I don't like that you got to know most every card in the game by heart before you can start REALLY playing.  Other games knowing the deck helps, but you can make smart decisions without it - and you can stand a chance against an experienced player.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Card-driven chaos.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Great game!
