RATING: 6
Nagyon jó az alapmechanizmus. Mind a splay, mint a coop/supremacy dogma hatalmas ötlet. Azt egy kicsit sajnálom, hogy ennyire kaotikus játékot építettek köré. Nem bánnám, ha egy kicsit kevesebb lenne a kitolós és több az építős akció, mert akkor tervezhetőbb lenne a játék. Az alapötlet szerintem jobb, mint a rómában, de az összhatás gyengébb a hatalmas káosz miatt. Ki kell próbálni hármasban is (vagy négyen csapatban), mert sok helyen írják, hogy a négyes kezelhetetlen.

Szimpatikus volt még, hogy annak ellenére, hogy elég absztrakt kártyajáték, nagyon tematikus tud lenni néhány lap hatása. Ha valaki előbb fedez fel valami nagy dolgot, akkor egyértelműen előnybe tud kerülni. Ahogy feltalálják a puskaport, úgy pusztulnak le a várak a világból stb.
8.1
------------
A harmadik játék után sajna visszább kell vennem az értékelést. Vannak benne egyértelműen, minden helyzetben nagyon erős, és nagyon gyenge kártyák, és ez eléggé nagy szerencse szerepet visz a játékba. Pl. a combustionnal gyakorlatilag egyedül meg lehet nyerni a játékot, míg sok olyan akár 7-8-as kártya van, ami lényegesen gyengébb, sok akár 1-esnél. Rómában is van hasonló, de ott simán beépíted a gyengébb darabokat nyersanyagnak, nomeg pörögnek a lapok, de itt simán beragad a kezedbe több gyenge is, mert aránylag nehéz húzni, és a gyengéket, se könnyű kiszórni.

Ezen felül négy fővel csak csapatban ajánlott a játék, mert különben akkora a káosz, hogy kezelhetetlen, és elkerülhetetlenül királycsinálás lesz a vége.
Illetve a spéci domination kártyákat annyira nehézre csinálták, hogy szinte lehetetlen megcsinálni őket.

Ambivalens hát az értékelés. Mert egyfelől semmihez se hasonló, spéci, absztrakt mechanizmussal sikerült egy nagyon tematikus játékot csinálni, de közben vannak bosszantó apróságok, amik beárnyékolják a játékmenetet.

-> 7

Nem jó ez a játék. Full random, full hullámvasút, látszatdöntésekkel. Kár érte, mert zseniális alapötletekre épül. Kéne Chudyk mellé valaki, aki rendbeteszi a játékait.

-> 6

RATING: 7
Innovation's card effects can be outrageously strong, even if compared with the already fairly ridiculous effects in Glory to Rome. That certainly makes the game more chaotic, and large swings of fortune are the norm rather than the exception. It may sound contradictory, but at the same time it is very hard to come back once you have fallen behind a bit too much.

If you're willing to accept that at times the winds of change will simply blow you away with nothing to do about it the game can emanate a certain crisp charm that other games of its class lack. Plotting the best course of action in an anbundance of ridiculous effects is at any rate a challenge, and while I'm not sure there is a way to win there is certainly more than one way to lose.

RATING: 6
Rating after 1/5/10/25 plays: 8+/8/6/-
[i]Nov 2014[/i]: Adjusted rating to 6.
[i]August 5, 2011:[/i] First impressions.
A brilliant game, has a slight edge over Race for the Galaxy (and this coming from a RftG and sci-fin fan). Don't let the 'simple and 'clip-arty' art fool you. In fact, the simple iconography makes it easy to scan and count the icons in play at any time. A meaty game that plays quickly (under an hour, and likely around 30 minutes for veterans). I felt I've played an epic civ game despite the short play time.

Looks like this is best with 3 players, OK with 2, but probably too chaotic with 4.

This is a sorely under-rated game (7.3). This is a solid '8' and definitely has the potential to reach a '9'.


[i]August 8, 2011:[/i] Tried the solitaire rules by GameRulesforOne. Quite challenging but not impossible to beat the solitaire engine, "The Ruling Party".

A solid 8+. This game is on track for being upgraded to a "9".

RATING: 2
I am trying to figure out how this is more than a sloppy, slow, take-that card game.

The problem is that there is no long term planning. Everything is so volatile that if you have anything that can be exploited, you just exploit it until it doesn't work anymore. If someone else has something better than you, you try to get as many cards in your hand so that you can break another player's exploit.
You can't even rely upon finding a particular card since one is removed from most eras at the start of the game.

Also, counting symbols is a nightmare.

RATING: 7

RATING: 8
After a bunch of plays, I'm still not sure about how much strategy there is.  Knowing the cards is an advantage, but not really necessary to play well.  Instead, there are some basic tenets that should be followed, and even then a new player can pull out a win.  I enjoy playing the game a lot, but I'm not sure if it's more Cosmic Encounter or Glory to Rome (meaning I don't know if varying card powers lends itself more towards unbalanced, Ameritrashy goodness or towards balanced strategic/tactical choices). 

Innovation works best as a two-player game.  More players make dogma actions more powerful, thus incentivizing them, and more dogma actions prevent strategy (or add randomness), which I think is best avoided.

RATING: 5
Waaaay better than glory to rome.  enjoyable card game that played very well with 2 in my only play.

After my third play, my opinion of the game has dropped drastically.  Yes, you're never out of the game because it can swing so wildly with just one great card.  Yes, it has some innovative mechanics.  

But: it's impossible to read the other player's cards and so it's too easy to slip a card onto your board one turn and surprise the player with it the next.  I don't want my opponent to read me the text on every card since that would extend the game far too long.  I can't imagine playing this with more than 3 players, and I don't think I even want to play it with 2.

RATING: 9
A marvel of game design!

Innovation is an outstanding, intense game for two players. It features a rapidly-changing game state that is full of surprises and allows for creative, clever, and multifaceted moves. But unlike most games with this level of chaos, Innovation is a very think-y, gamer-y game. The more you play it, the more you'll see the scope of the strategy and planning. It's the opposite of something like The Princes of Florence, where you carefully map out a long sequence of moves that will last you until the end of the game. In Innovation, your plans are constantly changing. Adapt and improvise, or die.

With three or four, I have trouble tracking what's happening on the other players' tableaus, so Innovation plays as more of a fast, loose, crazy, chaotic, un-serious game at that scale. Which is still fun, but I do prefer two players.

With four, use the partnership rules: partners play independently and must earn a total six achievements between them. "I demand" card effects do not apply to your partner. Other card effects apply normally, but you do not get the bonus card for sharing if your partner is the only one who partakes.

RATING: 7

It's nice that there's no one strategy that you can reliably fall back on. Every game is different.

RATING: N/A
Gian
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Innovation is an interesting engine building game. Cards can be played to your sets, determining draw ability and action ability or potentially scored as influence to meet certain win conditions. There are many cards in the game and each on has a different ability. Fortunately, each card draw is generally an action, so players rarely have too many cards in their hands. This makes decisions easier, but also very limiting. In this way, the game feels to play itself. There is little opportunity to counteract a powerful card elsewhere on the table, and if you receive one such card, there is little reason not to activate it as often as possible. Additionally, the mechanics are simple, but how to do each mechanic is over complicated. What is essentially a draw/play to tableau game is skewed by over-complicated methods. I can see how someone could enjoy Innovation and seek out many plays, but the lack of agency and high obstacles to get into the "flow" are jarring. Innovation is unique, but in unappealing ways.

RATING: 6
I find myself playing this less and less.  The mechanics of the game are interesting, but there's just not enough reactive player interaction to hold my interest.  By [i]reactive player interaction[/i] I mean the ability to react to the strategy that my opponent is playing.  My opponent has leaf superiority.  What can I do about that?  Well, nothing really, except hope that I pull lots of leaf cards or hope that I pull the right splay-dogma card or whatever.  I can't react to the game's state in a strategic way.

So if Innovation isn't strategic, perhaps it has interesting tactics?  Well, not usually.  The problem here is that card flow is so slow, and actions per turn are so few, that the tactical options open to you are usually very limited.  So, limited interesting tactical decisions, even fewer strategic decisions... why am I playing this game again?

RATING: 7.5
A finely-crafted game. It's got chaos and control, very unique mechanics (splaying is thoroughly original, the "sharing" is extremely fresh) and slightly more traditional ones (hand-management and throughput concerns), quick playing time, lots of interaction... 
If you hate games with lots of (manageable) player interaction, Innovation isn't for you--but otherwise, it is. Belongs in everyone's collection for its uniqueness and variety.

RATING: 4
This game is by the same designer as Glory To Rome, and it shows.  The cards change the rules dramatically, and often affect each other in big ways.  But cards are in stacks, and only the power of the top card in the stack takes effect, so it's much worse than Glory To Rome -- the rules can change too quickly.  For example, you may think that you have a plan for your next turn, but someone may steal a card from the top of your stack, or force you to play a card on top of it, thus removing its power and ruining your plan.

I guess if I knew the cards better, I could plan better and so I might like the game better.  But it is worth it when I could be playing something else?

A tableau building game in which players manage their hands, tableaux and score piles in order to accumulate achievement points.  Players draw from ten incremental decks, play cards to their tableaux, try to establish dominance in six symbols and execute actions ("dogma") which are unique to each card.  The mechanics interact well.  Actions can affect other players significantly.  Some of the actions involved at the tenth level seem to be a bit extreme.

RATING: 9
At first play, Innovation felt like a hot mess. There were symbols everywhere, loads of text, it wasn't clear as to how you might go about applying and maintaining leverage, or how you'd respond to your opponent's constant jabs. I still feel like it's a hot mess, but a Buster Bluth "hot mess": this is all-over-the-place in a good way. The game is not chaotic but rather creative with a grand  variety of tools at your disposal. After some games, there becomes a method to the madness, a narrative amongst seemingly non-causal relationships. And that's when the fun really begins. Do you tech up, score run, do you vie for an early leaf rush, deal in tableau-disruption, establish splaying comboes? The cards become your tools but don't fully dictate your play.

Furthermore, as abstract as the game is (for a game that is Civ-themed), the individual cards and their abilities mesh rather well. Pottery, for example, greatly represents the longstanding tradition of breaking pots (i.e., returning cards) for ceremonial purposes (i.e., scoring points) to enrich the soil which you would harvest later (i.e., draw a card). Don't get me started about how well some of the cards, and the ideas mesh. The famous drunk monk strategy (i.e., fermentation + reformation)? Totally Trappist.

RATING: 9
The best gamey game I know.  Also, I've probably played this more than any other game during my adulthood.

Innovation is topical and not experiential in a concrete sense.  You will not feel like you are guiding a civilization.  However, the game does have something to say about each component Innovation, whether it be trite or otherwise.  For instance, the medical innovations tend to be as aggressive as they are yellow.  There's something to think about there.  

It is a game that requires literacy and creative critical thinking.  I wonder how lawyers feel about the game and whether those in the Constructionist camp might appreciate the game more or less than those who are in a different sort of woods.  

The presentation of the version I have feels very adult in a cross-boundary-beyond-the-standard-gamer game way.  I do not think the game would have the same appeal with a different presentation.  

The expansions (green and blue are all I can type of) are worthwhile for the consistent player but not necessary for appreciation.  I prefer to play with everything, these days, but can understand how a more competitive player would prefer to stick with the basic set and be satisfied.

The game is exploratory within the constrains of the contained game field.  That exploration is where I get my pleasure.  Thus, I have yet to find another game that better suits that desire.  

The one draw-back is the lack of dialogue pertaining to the broader subject of innovation and human experience.

RATING: 9.8
It starts super slow and after a little playtime you will start to develop like when a bomb explode. The gameplay can be a bit chaotic (it is 100% guaranteed with more than 2 players). I like the game mechanic, love the multi use cards in such a clever way. The numerous endgame conditions are also welcome to go with. The iello's edition theme and artwork is outstanding. Carl Chudyk made another great game that i want to play. However i like to play Glory to Rome and Race For The Galaxy so much more and this is just because of those elegant gameplay.

Pros / Cons:
-Innovative game mechanic feels fresh right out of the box.
-The cards are in high interaction with each other which is almost always welcome.
-Because of the numerous endgame conditions you always have a slight chance to come back and win. So even if you feel like loosing just remember to never give up!
-Outstanding artwork in relation of the cool theme.
-Great price/value with the needed replayability.

-The chaotic gameplay sometimes destroys the fun. Im missing the elegant gameplay of Glory To Rome or Race For The Galaxy from this one.
-The interaction between the players can be irritating, especially when your enemy doing dogmas you can't stop.
-I feel balance issues which can be easily solved if the game went through much more playtesting.

RATING: 8.25
A game with all the trademarks of a Chudyk game: multiple paths to victory, dynamically unbalanced, play verging on crazy, really clever game mechanism.

On the basis of two solo plays, I'm not sure I love this one quite as much as I love GtR, but it's got huge potential for similar madcap play, and I definitely think it will be rising with additional plays.

The one complaint so far is that there can be a bit of a runaway leader factor, but I wouldn't expect that to happen too frequently, due to the many sneaky engines possible with different board combinations.

Increased to 7.5 after 5 solo plays- I NEED to teach this one to my daughter.

Increased to 8 after 10 solo plays- better than 7.5!

RATING: 2
(07/13/2010) Another card game where each card is multiple things, from the designer that brought you "Glory to Rome". Ugh. Glory to Rome was frustrating for the first handful of plays, but I continue to not get overly excited playing it. I was not overly excited about playing this.

I now know why I can't stand these types of games, because they require knowledge of a number of unique cards, each which has unique capabilities. This means playing the game a number of times to get familiar with what is in the deck.  Glory to Rome, Agricola, and Le Havre all make me suffer with having to know each card (and its unique mechanic) if I am to play well. While Dominion has considerably more cards to have knowledge of, it introduces them to me ten at a time, and the same ten cards are repetitively played over and over within the same game session, thus allowing me to rapidly familiarize myself with them. If I overlook a card in the other games, I will clearly be inducing sub-optimal play on myself, and I will not know about it until an hour or two later. This is frustrating.

Anyway, the text, numbers and iconography soon had me regretting getting in on this game. The theme was totally lost on me, as this felt like flowchart optimization. I will avoid it like the plague the next time, as I am not willing to invest the time and energy to play this effectively. I am just not feeling the love. It's a "4".

Update (01/26/11): Who am I kidding? Never going to play this again. It's a "2".

RATING: 9.89
Fifty plays into Innovation, I finally got it. I got how to manipulate the deck, how to make it go toward where I wanted, when to rush and when to score and when to meld. And I started winning like crazy. Because playing Innovation is like riding a sandworm. You get your hooks in a big one, you let it take you where it wants you to want it to go until it gets tired, then you dismount gracefully and search out another. And if you get your hooks in wrong, you’ll be chewed and spat. Get a good set in the worm and you keep riding, as long as your tableau has ride in it.

Just watching a game of Innovation is bewitching: splayed colors move left and right and up, shift from hand to score pile and back again, trade boards with seeming freedom. Playing Innovation means building up your icon stock, then throwing a rule into the works that you’ve saved back until you had the icons to support it. And then you ride that rule until somebody else’s rule throws you. Maybe till your own does. Ideally, you set up rules in all five colors and then start enforcing them like a lady with a beating stick.

It’s like having a brawl on the outside of a launching Saturn V. All game long you try to squeeze advantage out of landscape that constantly redefines itself. You can be overpoweringly strong, then someone gets a tech advantage on you and turns your tech level into a liability. Sure, you want to reach higher tech eras, but you also want to be buying achievements, because a win on score if the tech eras are rushed out is an embarrassing way to go. You really can win from almost any position in Innovation, but knowing the cards—even more so the eras—is the only foundation for doing so. The more you play, the more the chaos diminishes, and the more the game becomes a finely controlled dance, seemingly chaotic to those who don’t understand the context, beautiful to those who do. Innovation is [thing=589]Wiz War[/thing] for cultural anthropologists.[color=#ffffff]a9Z4MnAx[/color]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.6
Innovation es un juego con cartas, hermano de Gloria a Roma, en el que se "desarrolla" la civilización humana de manera muy abstracta y condensada en 1h. de juego con 105 cartas para representar el recorrido a través del árbol tecnológico humano (desde la agricultura y la rueda hasta la bomba nuclear).

El objetivo se logra principalmente dominando eras (varía su nº en función de los participantes). Para ello, hay que llevar cartas a nuestra zona de puntuación y estos puntos se usarán para lograr dominar determinada era (10 en cada partida desde la prehistoria hasta la edad de la información). A razón de 5 puntos por nº de era (15 para dominar la era 3, 25 para la era 5...)

Mecánicamente es muy sencillo, ya que hay cuatro acciones distintas de las cuales podemos hacer dos por turno (iguales o no), a saber: robar carta, jugar carta, dominar una era o ejecutar un dogma. Es en esta última dónde la cosa se complica. Con ella se activa el texto de la carta jugada en la mesa, semiobligando a los jugadores a hacer lo que en ella se requiere. Y hay 105 cartas. Todas distintas. 

Me encanta la mecánica de "arrastrar". Las cartas se van jugando y apilando en función de los 5 colores que tiene, y cuando se "arrastra", fruto del efecto de la otra carta, desplazas el apilamiento, de tal forma que dejas visible parte de las cartas inferiores, mostrando más iconos que te harán lograr más recursos..

Pros: La mecánica del arrastre. Ingeniosísima. Que dependas de tu fortaleza en determinada habilidad para poder aprovecharte de las acciones de los otros cuando activan sus dogmas. La progresión a través de las cartas y sus poderes/dogmas que te permiten ir haciendo mejores jugadas y combos (si te gustan los combos, este es tu juego). El uso de las cartas para multitud de cosas (son eras, puntos, recursos o poderes en función de dónde/cómo estén colcocadas). Interacción contínua sin llegar a ser puteante. Edición muy cuidada de Homoludicus que mejora la original. Un juego para 2 que es profundo (no debería probarse a +3 jugadores, a no ser que te guste el Double Fanucci). 

Cons: La sensación que lo único que haces es adaptarte a lo que te ha tocado es total. Hay demasiado azar y situaciones cambiantes, lo que hace muy improbable poder jugarlo con algo de estrategia. Muy abstracto con el tema de la civilización absolutamente pegado, ya que no suele haber relación entre el nombre de la tecnología que has jugado y su efecto en la partida. Iconos por todas partes. Conocer 105 cartas para poder jugar decentemente. Parece que estas constantemente contando: símbolos, puntos, multiplicando x5, leer las cartas de los oponentes, leer las propias cartas, no sé si todo esto deja tiempo para disfrutar de una partida. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I like this far more as a two player game than a three or four player game.

This game is a great Chudyk game, which means plenty of chaos, a lot of interactions between different abilities on cards, and a mistaken impression that a given combo is imbalanced. It's not imbalanced, you just haven't found the right counter for it yet.

I like Innovation a lot, and it's up there with Red7 as my current favorite from Carl Chudyk's ouevre. Two player games play quickly, are deeply strategic, and can have massive come from behind victories. You are very rarely completely out of the running in a game of Innovation, and there are interesting moments where early game cards are suddenly stronger in late game than they ever were in the early game.

I wish I got to play this more, since it seems to really reward extensive replays. Might need to look into setting up a partner to really get into this.

RATING: 7
I find this game really interesting and surprisingly simple despite all of the (seemingly) complicated terminology. I like how quickly the game plays, I like the ideas, I really like the clean, simplistic artwork.

What I am less enthusiastic about is the snowball effect. Usually the first person to get the upper hand is the winner. Still, I'm giving it a rather high rating, because I feel that there is more to explore within the game and that it is quite possible that what I don't really like disappears with becoming a more experienced player.

Overall: a really good, fast, simple and original game.

RATING: 7.7
Apr10:

Pretty solid game.  Everyone just loves getting to the highest tech levels and look at the crazy powerful cards.  I mean, if nothing else, you should play it just to get a chance to see what's in the '10' deck.

How is the actual game?  A little confusing at first honestly.  I mean, it plays smooth eventually, but you're just sorta playing along at first.

It takes a fair amount of deck familiarity to get some strategy goin'.

I don't quite like the stutter of "count and see who can follow" but it's not really that bad.

Overall, very successful.  We'll see if we can get more strategy in the game with more plays.

Mar11:

After ~10 more plays? I have more respect for it... maybe. I still think it pales in comparison to Race for the Galaxy. Too much chaos and too little planning. Still, as long as it's played swiftly, I enjoy it. If a player tries to min/max too much, they just kill the game. And counting the icons still blows.


We did try the 2v2 variant in the last game. This was infinitely better than the only other 4 player game I've tried. I would never play regular 4-player again.

For me, there really only seems to be two solid strategies though:

A. Run up the tech tree as fast as you can. So many of the 10s are just "I Win" that you generally just, well, win.

B. Get to factories-ish and ramp your score quickly. Make sure you steal some to slow the opponent down. Snag a bunch of achievements and win.

I realize those are sort of general, but you almost do need to pick one of those plans to go for I'd say. If you dally with castles, you will almost definitely lose.

May12:

I don't have a clue where I came up with the above comments.  This game can be a chaotic mess, but still be fun as long as you keep going at a swift speed.

RATING: 6
initial rating

Hmmm, on the surface it looks like this game was overhyped (what a shock...not) and not unlike other [i]recent[/i] games of this Ilk, a bit of a pattern that...

Cool combos and all that but not sure how much depth is really there, its all tactics really as things change quite a bit and cards always come out randomly from face down piles.  Though cards are all different or unique, MOST do a lot of the same things, ie throw a card away and score a point, throw 2 cards away score 2 points, etc, etc.  Still, I’m a card game whore and this still seems like a fun one overall. Nuclear bomb, WOW is all I have to say about that. Is splaying even worth it? Must be I suppose if you get enough symbols, I do need to play more to suss things out better.   

At any rate, I’m not sure I care about the chaos yet and those that know me know I hate it, so far, I never really seen it really just very small doses so once I do, its probably going to suck no doubt but apparently some of the chaos can be overcome? lets us see about that.  Luck of the draw seems to be here in spades too but still, I want to play more to see if there is anymore more than meets the eye as some seem to let on to...(note it also has that annoying Le Havre effect where you need to look across the whole table to try and read what everyone else has and it's nearly impossible to do with all the small text on cards) I'm not all that inspired to keep playing so far and will definitely avoid 4 player games

UPDATE:  Played this again, only the 2nd time now and 3 years later (yeah wasn't too excited about this one, but I did pick it up to explore a little since so many people seem to like it) and I am of the same mind as my comments 3 years ago, overhyped. Looking at my comments I wonder if I was taught slightly wrong originally (splaying would always be worth it it seems now) but it doesn't change anything in the big picture. It's easy to see the appeal, but holy shit the chaos here even playing with 2 players. Lose your hand, lose all your top cards on board, player steals your cards and scores them, and on and on. So while there is some good things I don't see how they outweigh the bad here since you have very limited control. 

So bottom line I'd rather player Race for the Galaxy, GtR or a host of other card games when given the choice. I should also note the theme is pretty weak really and can be completely ignored when playing (I suppose you could "look for it" too and see it there, but its just not important to gameplay at all). But let's see, I'll give it at least one more shot since I now own it, it seems there is probably enough chaos that maybe it evens out, but between that and the luck of the draw I don't know how wins can be meaningful really. Yet some people say with enough play you can actually play more strategically, and surf this crazy chaos (while I could see this, I'm not sure I'll have the patience to get through that many plays and the card effects here are sometimes SO strong that I still think control has to be fairly limited) Rating likely to drop to 6 or even 5 if not enough control can be exerted. 

P.S. - once again I love Morgan's comments, this one is for a "shorter variant" capturing the essence of the gameplay here: (thanks for the laugh again Morgan!)

[q=sisteray]In a three player game, randomly deal out a 10 card, an 8 card and a 4 card. The person with the 4 card gets to trade for the 10 card and wins. The old 10 card guy comes in last even though he had the high number because ultimately he drew the wrong card and someone else drew the right one. [/q]

UPDATE: 7 => 6 Gave it another chance, opponent got Gunpowder which I eventually countered but it put it me behind, okay fine, but then they got Statistics. This card ended the game, its WAY too strong and if you can't counter it because LUCK did/does not give you the right card in the draw, you're totally fubared. "[i]I demand you transfer the highest card in your score pile to your hand![/i]" So there was no point for me to try and score, since opponent would just make me pick up the card, and without scoring you can not achieve thus can't win the game. I picked up about 15 cards looking for a counter, got none. (Though there are counters there somewhere, maybe I should toast Statistics from the deck completely...edit, yeah the more I think about it, the more almost broken that one card seems, its a game-ender without a counter)

It seems there are many of these types as I mentioned before, don't see how you can plan for them if that many, you don't know which ones you will get vs opponent, so its just pure chaos really. I can see why people like the game for the neat aspects like splaying and combos, but I don't get how the chaos and luck does not bother them. Ah well, likely give it one more chance, but my patience is the near the end for sure, chaos and lack of control is one of my most, if not the most detested features in a game.

RATING: 8
== Preliminary comments after 2 plays. It's an 8 for sure, with that value likely to rise.

My wife and I played twice on the night that Innovation arrived. It's a good game, but I'll bet that the 4-player game is crazy chaotic.

There's a lot going on, lots of small combos (and some bigger ones), and a lot of decisions. Tina crushed me in the first game, but the second one was much more back and forth, with the lead changing several times. Hand / score pile / tableau management is front-and-center, and most of your decisions revolve around managing one of the areas to maximize a particular effect.

We didn't make it into Age 8, so none of the insanity of the Age 9 / 10 cards came into play. Those ages are basically two stacks of bombs that give someone who is behind in achievements (the primary win condition) a chance to win on one of the secondary conditions.

I think that much like Glory To Rome there will be a point within the first dozen plays where things click and you start to play your opponent more. In GtR the first step in the learning curve is figuring out that you have to manage the pool to prevent your left-hand opponent from scooping up a disproportionately good set of clients.

In Innovation there are many times per game where you have to choose between advancing your own agenda and attacking others, between pushing the ages and dumping cards back into the supply to slow things down.

I'm looking forward to exploring this.

== Note regarding the concerns about Gunpowder / Engineering being overpowered.  I just don't see it. Sure, you could be dumb and load up on castles going into Age III and then watch your entire civilization get leveled. On the other hand, you could simply not be dumb. The castle cards are quite powerful and attractive early. Use some, but don't go overboard, unless you accept the risk involved in doing so.

RATING: 6.5
2013-01-23

Initial Rating: 6.5 (August 2012)

This game has been a roller-coaster for me. At first, as I really like Glory to Rome, I was expecting a lot from Innovation and I was disappointed in the game. I found the game to be mostly luck and chaos, however with each play I found more control hidden between the chaos and I’m liking it more and more. I still think the game has a lot of luck, but perhaps it has more to offer that I initially thought. I only have played with four players (free for all) so I believe as a 2 player game the chaos can go down considerably and it would open the door to skillful play. (I hope)

As with Glory to Rome, it appears the game has a steep learning curve. It has a ton of cards and each one has a different text and effect, so experience counts a lot in this game. I really like Civ theme games, however, when I play Innovation I don’t feel it (I’m only looking for icons, dogmas and colors) so the theme is mostly lost on me.

The game can take a lot of space for a card game. The components quality is ok and the art of the cards is also ok. The art serves well its purpose. (But the components are much nicer in the Spanish edition).

For now the jury is still out on this one and I want to play again soon to really appreciate the game.

RATING: 9
Chaotic, churning fun, so if your idea of the perfect game is to quietly gather resources and build a little optimisation engine more efficiently than, and untroubled by, the other players then this is categorically not your type of game.

But if wild swings of fortune and the ability to pounce on the others (metaphorically, of course!) is up your street then there's a good chance this is too.

Seems simpler than the designer's earlier 'Glory to Rome' which I didn't expect.

The Iello production is stunning for what remains a relatively inexpensive card game. Gone are the clip art graphics of the original, and the physical quality of the box in particular is superb - it's closed by a magnet!

RATING: 3

Another of those games that is great if you don't have a strategic interest and just want to see how things play out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The artwork and play are a more sophisticated (therefore  less "fun") Glory to Rome. This game derives from rummy rather than trick-taking. Some features of two games are superficially similar: cards with multiple functions, "super-duper" card powers (here called "dogmas", and which get more powerful as the game progresses through seven different decks) and produce a playful chaos. You still tuck accumulated cards under different sides of a mat. 
The game has some innovative mechanics (my favorite of which is "splaying", i.e. spreading out melded card piles in one of three directions in order to tactically gain the technologies on them), but the jury is still out overall. How does one overcome the fatal flaw of being unable to read the print upsidedown on opponents' cards? You can't really memorize 140 powers; and, unfortunately, they "make or break" your game. GtR only has 40, and SJ even fewer, so you can actually plan to counter your opponent.  
On the other hand, this game feels like a refreshingly new traditional card game (my favorite genre), and I wonder if over time I'll find more of a sublime, mature pleasure here. The mechanism of stacking a bunch of a cards in a pile and then splaying them upon your opponents is delicious and forces you to think in whole new ways! It's one of those mechanisms that feels so natural you think should be a thousand years old.

RATING: 9
I've had mixed feelings about combo card games like Race to the Galaxy and Glory to Rome. As in, I tend to like them in theory more than reality. However, Innovation is the first card game of this type that I can without reservation say that I enjoy. I more than enjoy it actually. I think Innovation tops the others for me because it is more streamlined. While it uses symbology, it does not leverage it to the degree of complexity that can be a barrier to teaching some of these other games. The actions are simple, the special actions clearly explained, and the power-ups logical and clear. I think this solves many of my issues with the earlier games in this genre. I can more quickly ramp up here to simply enjoy the game, and that I have done. The possibilities are rich with (mostly) tactical but also (some) strategic options. For two players, this is really excellent stuff. Not sure I'd be interested in playing it with more...

RATING: 5
Rating based on about 6-7 plays.  Doubt it will change much.  If you take the game seriously, it's like Race for the Galaxy, in that there's a steep learning curve and you really have to know all the cards (105 of them).  There's a tremendous amount of luck here, IMHO, in having the right cards at the right time.  While I really wanted to like this game, I think there's a fatal flaw which I call the "front runner problem," namely if a player chooses the "Achieve" action to claim a VP card, that player doesn't have to sacrifice anything from his/her scoring pile and is the front runner for obtaining the next VP card.  It's up to the game system and the other players to do something to bring the leader down, perhaps sacrificing their own positions, plus they might not have the right cards to do this.  The one game I actually won was a game I was out of it, had ZERO achievements and very few cards in my meager scorepile, and then another player who was behind but much closer to the leader than I was did a "Hail Mary," flipped a card that said the player with the most leafs (me) wins.  Wow, what a game!

RATING: 10
Unrated after two plays, a 7 after four, an 8 after five, a 9 after six, and a 10 after 30. Best with two players, great with three, and fun as a partnership game with four. 

Innovation is the follow-up to Glory to Rome, and as such it has some very big shoes to fill. There are ~100 unique cards spread over 10 periods of human history, from Prehistory to the Information Age. The complexity and range of the card-set is wonderful; the graphic design is not. Relevant text is tiny, making it unnecessarily difficult to keep track of your opponents' capabilities (a key aspect of the game). The impact of any given card hints at its thematic corollary, but the level abstraction is high or at least 'feels' quite high initially. With more play, those connections become clearer, but whether they become "clear enough" will be a problem for many players. The GtR fanbase is already split on this. 

The biggest potential flaw in the gameplay itself is the scoring. To win the game, players must claim "achievements" by accumulating points in their score pile. Often, the player who claims the first achievement is best positioned to claim the second - a classic breakaway leader problem. Essentially what it amounts to is that players don't have to pay anything to score, they just have to have the cash on hand. Fortunately, the opportunity cost to scoring seems sufficiently high for the competition to take a step back, reorganize, and then sprint ahead with much greater speed. That takes skill and familiarity, though, so early games may see one player claim the first 5 achievements in sequence, winning handily while the other players never get off the starting line. Such a result is profoundly unsatisfying. 

The other odd thing about Innovation is that players only get two actions in a round, and the basic versions of these actions are very weak. Players will need to think [i]very[/i] carefully about their options and find ways to leverage their cards that are 2-4 times as productive as a basic move. The disparity between basic moves and power plays makes it easy for players to feel utterly stalled from time to time. That's not very satisfying either, lending a "tightrope" quality to the initial, disorienting plays. 

I'm convinced there's a great game here, and honestly I'd have bet against it after my first two plays. It will take a [i]long[/i] time to become familiar with each "Innovation" in the deck, but if that's acceptable there's no reason not to explore and enjoy this incredible game. 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3.5
I can see how this could be decent or even fun if you had only one opponent. I'm sure it's good for two. But you know how sometimes, when you're a teenager, you fill up too much on one kind of liquor one night, and for the rest of your life, no matter how good it might be, the smell of tequila makes you a little bit nauseous? 

See, I'm having the same problem here as I had with Erosion (a not-that-dissimilar game). The box says you can play with 2-4 people, but playing it with four is a hideous mistake. Do I say what I really think about it after that one play, since I'll never play it again? I think it's fair game - if you don't want your product reviewed after a play with four, then don't market it as a game that'll accommodate four.

You might be able to tell where I'm going with this.

First of all, the AP is rife. In theory, you should know what everyone's cards are at all times. In theory, you should be keeping track of what's in the discard pile. In theory, you should be able to make it more difficult for your opponents to screw you with Dogma cards. And in theory, everyone should get a reasonable shot at splaying, score cards, and so on.

In practice, with four anyway, none of these things are really possible, though you might be able to manage the first two if you don't mind waiting half an hour between turns.

With four, there are so many chances for people to mess with your cards that you can't plan anything from turn to turn. I didn't get any splay or tuck or score cards for the first six turns of the game, so I ended up way behind with no way to catch up except by using my leaf dogma to grab the occasional 2- and 3-value card. This is the classic problem for games like this - once you fall far enough behind, what do you do? Throw the game? Just draw cards and wait for whoever's ahead to deliver a headshot? That's probably more interesting than trying to spend twenty minutes calibrating futile moves. 

It gets points for being pretty to look at, but- seriously? There was no way to pattern the cards so that I can easily see at a glance what their basic function is, like whether they're used to Demand or to Meld or whatever? 

There are 140 cards, every single one with a different power, so you'd have to play at least ten or twenty games to get to know what's out there and how it works with everything else. If this sort of game is what puts lead in your pencil, then go for it. I won't.

RATING: 4
Okay firstly:  VERY COOL and indeed, innovative overall gameplay.  The "splaying", the 10 ages laid out in a circle, the scoring and achieving - all good stuff.

RATING: 7
Great game with some similarities to Glory to Rome. Very high level of interaction with some very powerful combinations possible. Seems to be an extremely tactical game, as you need to react to what is going on and do the best with what you have available with a few strategic decisions mixed in. Played with 2 & 3 so far with 3 being my preference. If the game goes into ages 9 or 10, be prepared for some serious craziness. Although I do not know if this was the plan, seems that future expansions could certainly be possible.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.2

With Glory to Rome, crazy was the new norm.  Innovation is Glory to Rome crazy x2 with more polish and a tighter theme.

I love the numerous complex and hidden math mechanics.  The 'splaying' mechanic is genius!

Adopting the video game practice of selling the beta for wide play testing with the promise of a 1.0 update for the early adopters is a model that small boardgame publishers should utilize. Genius!

My only beef is that the game is to short for such juiciness!

[Edit]  I fission-ed the game today.  As you might expect, it was twice as long.  Now I want the game to have more cards, but especially more cards in the early eras while maintaining the ability to jump to a new era with a dogma effect. Result: more re-playability/combination/mystery with the early cards.

Each player builds a civilization based on various technologies, ideas, and cultural advancements, all represented by cards. Each of these cards has a unique power which will allow further advancement, point scoring, or even attacking other civilizations. Be careful though, as other civilizations may be able to benefit from your ideas as well!

RATING: 6
[2010.Dec.03] Another "civilization lite" tech development game. This card-based affair has a relatively high complexity due to the numerous, unique cards that need to be "known" in order to be able to play the game with any proficiency. Additionally, overall game balance is at the mercy of the luck of the draw, because some cards' effects obviously outshine the effects of others. This is more of an experience game, than an actual game.

RATING: 3
Played three times, 3p, 4p.  Not nearly fast enough to be light, way too random to be heavy.  This game occupies a very overcrowded niche, with several better games.  To play this game fast, you'll need to play several times in succession, to cement the mechanics and the different card interactions; until then, games will take longer than you'd like.  Since you have very little control over the cards you draw and/or can use (though you do have some control), randomness haters will hate the game.  Better games in this category include Glory to Rome and 7 Wonders.  Gets a pip up for the innovative (heh) use of the icon as the resource.  I have read that it plays better as a 2p game (I believe it).  I have also read that it needs several games to reduce the randomness - this may be true, but I don't care.  The game isn't fun enough the first dozen times to justify the 13th, when there are hundreds, if not thousands, of games that ARE fun those first dozen times.

RATING: 3
This review is based on reading the rules and starting a solo game for testing, playing a few turns and stopping before my head exploded.

This game is a real brain burner. But if you like games like race for the galaxy, you should be able to handle it.

The good points of these games is that there are some interesting mechanics, like the icon and the sliding of cards. Second, the fact that the card special ability have REAL text is essential.

The first problem is that the rules are not clearly written because most of the rules are on the cards. For example, they tell you where is your score pile, and what you can buy with your score, but you do not know how to place cards in your score pile. So when you start the game, you have many holes in your head.

The main problem is that it is very complex. Each card has a special ability, you can use 1 or 2 of them on your turn. The problems is that all abilities consist in moving and drawing cards on the board. So you cannot guess with a single glance what the card does. 

That first create the problem that analyzing the cards you have and determining which are the best cards to keep, which is going to be placed into play and which you are going to use this turn is overwhelming. 

The seconds problem is that you lose track of the theme, if you had an ability like "Metal working: increase your military strength", you actually know and can visualize that your army is getting stronger. But in the game, you will move cards around without really having an idea of what has happened. You could use a card bad for you and not know it. 

But that is not enough, it gets even more complex because all card abilities can/must be used by other players when use them according. So if they have more or less icons of a certain type than you into play, they have to play the same action as you before you. That becomes very complex, because you need to analyze the icons of the other player too and see the impact on their game play. This is where the head simply explodes.

Finally, the box is so big. Common, it's a deck of card with some chart. It would have fit in a box with 1/3 of the thickness and you would have had space for and expansion deck.

I understand that in these kind of card games, you need to have a very flexible game to make sure you can have many unique cards, but it's just too much.

RATING: 7
An unsupecting good little game on the development of human society.  Some very good card gaming design with intricate interactions.  I have yet to explore its full potential. 

Innovation is basically a mix and match card play in the very nature of the system. However, the mechanics make sense to what a player accomplishes in terms of the game objectives - the development of civilization to achieve 5 wonders. The cards are divided into 5 types and they are randomly dealt to you. Using what you have in hand, you try to maximize the developments on all fronts - from agriculture to monarchy, industrialization to economy. It is a clever design and offers some hours of enjoyment in deep strategy thinking. Overall, I like to play this game again and so far it is satisfying a game experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
(Some of the comments below come from impressions from the few games with beginners that I saw at Visite de Iello à Paris. Others come from actually playing the game.)

* Negative points:

- Players take a lot of time deciphering the card effects.  This makes the game more adapted to people who often play together.  Keeping all the various effects in mind is challenging: those of the cards in your Zone, those of the cards in Hand, and those of the cards in your opponents' Zones.  Magic is a little easier, because effects are more thematic.  However, after a couple of games, the situation is really not bad.

- It can be near impossible to stop certain cards: your influence does not grow fast enough (or even not at all), lags behind, you lag behind in ages, and your opponent has a very powerful card that you can do nothing about. (Note added later: I now think that in these conditions drawing and trying to move up quickly in the ages is a decent way out, as stronger powers come with later ages.) This is very different from a game like Race for the Galaxy, where one can still make victory points, implement a strategy, create new ones that bring more victory points, etc. In Magic, many cards can still usefully block the opponent's attacks: there is still hope, a fight, the possibility of defending oneself. Even in Glory to Rome, even when behind, one can still build buildings, amass material in one's vault, etc. Innovation can be a bit frustrating, not as much because victory seems inaccessible, but because it feels like almost nothing can be done about it, and there is sometimes almost no way of making any kind of progress (no card that gives influence, can stop the opponent, etc.). That said, maybe one finds himself in such uncomfortable situations only after making some strategic mistakes; such mistakes can be quite subtle, though, and I am still discovering them after more than 20 plays. In any case, this kind of situations is one of the very few blemishes of this game (Race for the Galaxy, Agricola do not have this kind of problem; maybe Space Hulk does, where a bad strategy can be hard to fix).

* Neutral points:

$ Some effects are not obviously thematic (Optics…).  However, most effects can be interpreted in some thematic way.

* Positive points:

+ The 2 player game is tense.  There are no extreme point swing (age 8-10 changes in the winning conditions are rare).

+ The race for Influence participates seriously to the tension. This is particularly tangible when some cards provide quasi "automatic scoring" (Medicine…).

+ The effects and the themes are varied. It is nice to "travel" this way (compared to more "uniform" games like some traditional card games, etc.).

+ The fact that there are only 5 card effects active in the Zone at a time helps devising tactics. However, there is still a lot to consider, because cards in the hand also have powers, and there is no hand limit (this may be mitigated by the fact that cards from the later periods are probably more powerful and can therefore be read in priority).  The game is satisfying.

+ The effects can often be interpreted in some way. Chemistry helps blue cards by splaying them: chemistry open possibilities in the technical domain, etc.

+ The rules are quite simple and streamlined. The FAQ helps smooth small details about the interpretation of the rules.

+ The player mats in the Iello edition are really convenient for new players, and as a refresher.

+ Easy to set up (provided that the Ages were sorted beforehand).

+ The mechanics of the dogmas are streamlined: weaker players suffer from dominations; players who are at least as strong also benefit from bonuses; the player with the dogma has a small advantage, since it emanates from his civilization. Easy to remember, and thematic.

+ It must feel nice to use a powerful effect, through a special combo.

+ New problems to be solved arise often: the game has variety.

+ Brain taxing: one has to constantly adjust to a shifting situation, and remember the effects of the cards that remain, but rethink them in the context of the new tableau cards, of the evolving influence and hand cards. More brain taxing than Race for the Galaxy, in this respect.

+ The number of answers to a problem, or of new strategic routes is often quite high: this allows player to display some satisfying creativity.

+ There is real tension, as interaction is quite high: other players can attack, and it is not always possible to answer immediately.

+ Turns are fast, or often have little downtime, with only 2, often simple actions, or supremacy/cooperative dogmas where other players often participate.

+ Some effects have a "dice" effect: some cards can stop being the Active one when a random card is put in play; this creates some nice Schadenfreude/yippee effects.

+ The game is not really chaotic, with 2 players, contrary to what I was led to believe initially from reviews.  In fact, my first two games had a clear strategic orientation for both players (fast influence lead vs fast age advancement).  Some effects are quite strong and game changing (Fission…), but the effects are on average less strong than in Magic: the Gathering, and they provide for some enjoyable drama.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Nota 1:
Arrastrar los mazos es quizá el factor más determinante del juego. Unos mazos arrastrados otorgan gran cantidad de recursos que permiten por un lado neutralizar los dogmas de supremacía del rival y por otro potenciar tanto los de supremacía propios como los de cooperación tanto propios como ajenos.

Nota 2: 
No hay carta mala; una situación favorable puede hacer que una carta inofensiva en otras circunstancias resulte letal. Como no hay límite a la cantidad de cartas que un jugador puede tener en la mano, es recomendable guardarlas ahí a la espera del momento más idóneo para su uso. 

Nota 3: 
Un equilibrio entre lo comentado en las 2 notas anteriores es la clave para la victoria en este juego.


MUY IMPORTANTE:

UN DOGMA DE COOPERACIÓN BENEFICIA A CUALQUIER JUGADOR RIVAL QUE AL HACER USO DE DICHO DOGMA MODIFICA EN ALGO LA SITUACIÓN DE LA PARTIDA: robar/descartarse, archivar/reciclar alguna carta...
SI NO ES EL CASO Y NINGÚN RIVAL REALIZA NINGUNA ACCIÓN QUE MODIFIQUE EL CURSO DE LA PARTIDA, SE CONSIDERA QUE DICHO DOGMA NO HA BENEFICIADO A NADIE Y POR TANTO EL JUGADOR QUE JUGÓ DICHO DOGMA NO RECIBE SU ACCIÓN EXTRA DE ROBAR UNA CARTA DEL MAZO DE ERA QUE CORRESPONDA.

DIFERENCIA ENTRE A QUIÉN AFECTA Y A QUIÉN BENEFICIA UN DOGMA COOPERATIVO:

UN DOGMA COOPERATIVO AFECTA A TODOS LOS JUGADORES CON UN Nº IGUAL O SUPERIOR DE LOS RECURSOS QUE INDICA EL DOGMA DEL JUGADOR QUE LO HA JUGADO. POR TANTO, TAMBIÉN AFECTA AL JUGADOR QUE JUEGA DICHO DOGMA.
EN CAMBIO, UN DOGMA COOPERATIVO SÓLO BENEFICIA A LOS JUGADORES RIVALES DEL JUGADOR QUE LO JUEGA Y QUE AL HACER USO DE DICHO DOGMA MODIFICAN EN ALGO LA SITUACIÓN DE LA PARTIDA.

RATING: 5
The theme looked interesting and we were eager to try to develop our civilizations with new and cool technologies.

However, we didn't really enjoy it.  The game felt chaotic and random, but also slow to start.  The beginning felt slow as we kept drawing cards and trying to meld them to get something useful started.  Conversely, technologies felt very fleeting as you played cards on top of them and covered them up.  At the same time, it felt like a lot of work to keep track of the number of icons we had (after every action, we had to keep counting to see if anything had changed and the other player got to use the action or not).  This became especially cumbersome once we had played quite a few cards.  Moreover, once one of us got a lead in one of the icons, it felt almost like a runaway since that player could active those actions over and over again while the other player could only try to draw cards to find something to counteract it.

I think partly what disappointed us is that we were expecting more of a resource engine game, whereas [thing=63888][/thing] is much more tactical and situational.  It was too random for us.  I think we prefer games where we build up an engine.  [thing=19857][/thing] had this feeling (and even more so [thing=28143][/thing], one of our favorite games in this genre), but [thing=63888][/thing] was not for us.  My wife in particularly reacted strongly and swore never to play it again.

RATING: 9
A brilliant civilization-themed tableau-building game.  Excruciating decisions, fast turns, little if any down time.  Such new and novel gameplay mechanics are introduced like "splay", "foreshadow", "tuck", "echo"--I have never before seen these in any card game.  I love the fluidity, the choices, and possible chaotic finishes.  This game seems infinitely replayable, especially with "Echoes of the Past" and "Figures in the Sand".  Warning:  can have significant direct conflict!

A side note:  I guess whether one classifies "evolution" as an innovation boils down to whether he or she considers it a man-made theory or a [discovered] natural process. 

Best with two or [i]perhaps[/i] three players.  Not recommended with 4.  There is too much information on the table as tableaus grow, and it can be difficult enough to keep track of your own player area.  TTFN.  -->  G4GG prize.

RATING: 8
Another entry in the illustrious list of games that manage to pack an astounding amount of game into a pack of cards. It is hard to point out what makes playing Innovation feel so unique and, well, innovative, but I’ll give it a try: you play out technology cards in five different colours, starting out with tech level 1. Your aim is to gain higher tech cards, both because of their improved abilities and of their point value, which can be used to gain achievements, a.k.a victory points (six achievements and you’re king of the hill). In a lovely twist, these achievements come from a common pool and have steeply increasing prices, right away giving you two strategic options in either buying early and cheap or focusing on your tech tree and scoring lage and big. 

Improving your technologies is a balancing act, because only the highest card of each of the five tech colours can be used for its effect - the others will possibly contribute power symbols that determine whether you can attack other players or benefit from their actions. The way in which these symbols are handled is very original, leads to a lot of interaction and breathtakingly dynamic gameplay and is bound to confuse the hell out of you during the first game. If you thought that Race for the Galaxy was too demanding, stay away, and regardless of that, listen well to the author’s recommendation of starting out with two players only.

Innovation is the most intriguing new game I’ve played in a couple of months, and one of the (regrettably) only two games I’ve played so far was a real nail-biter to boot, with both players at five achievements in the last round.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
(Thoughts in 2013 - 5 plays - rating 6.5):Take that, chaotic, and plays longer than it should.  I'll give it a few more tries to see if it improves once I can recognize card synergies.  Right now, I will not play this more than once a game session.  Too frustrating.

Update in January 2018: Got rid of Innovation not long after I originally rated it due to all the reasons above.  It showed up on boardgamearena so I decided to try it out again.  Way more enjoyable now.  Game is still a bit chaotic, involves quite a bit of take that, but it is much better once you start to try to manage your symbols and get a better feel for the cards in the deck.  Now I can see how tactical it is.  Offers plenty of opportunities for you to stop and think about how you can get the upperhand on your opponent.  I can see myself playing this 100+ times and not getting bored of the base game.  Will likely avoid the expansions.

RATING: 5
UPDATE: Downgrading from an 8 to 5.  While the mechanics are sound, sometimes you cannot get an engine going because of lousy card draws.  With lack of card mitigation, it can make the game very frustrating. 

Plays in under an hour and very easy to teach.  The only challenging part of the game is wrapping your head around the different dogmas effects.  The wording can be very tricky so if you are playing with people who are not used to crazy card effects like Magic the Gathering, expect plenty of questions heading your way about what the cards do.

Once you've got that nailed down, it's a decent game.  I wouldn't recommend it with four players since there is way too much information to keep track of.  Three player works okay, but the apex is at the two player count.

RATING: 9
Building a civilization around the discovery of key inventions, with an aim to dominate particular eras of history.

20/8/12 (7 plays) 

Awesome game - hasn't disappointed me yet.  Every play is more and more enjoyable and I discover different nuances to the cards.  

2 player games are fast, with a strong momentum factor.  4 player games are slower and more complex. 

Love it, glad I own it and doubt this will change any time soon.  Plus it's a quick fire and uncomplicated civilization game without many bits and pieces.  Plus it is super portable.  Definitely one of the best value games I own.

The art on the 2nd (Iello) edition is gorgeous and it is incredibly well presented.  My only (minor) gripe is that the reds and blues can be hard to distinguish for those who happen to be colour blind.

17/11/12:  Downgraded to a 9.5 from a 10 after 16 plays.  Even in a two player game, there is a bit of luck which can make or break a game.

1/1/13: Downgraded to a 9 due to randomness.  Still great, but some games can involve lucky runs that can't be countered by any amount of good play.  Not that the rise and fall of civilizations was ever meant to be fair.

RATING: 7
After the first play this feels like Fluxx: Extended Edition, but this is probably because we were all playing like idiots. (Verified after a few more plays that yes, we were.)

After 32 plays, this game is still chaotic and reminiscent of Fluxx, but unlike Fluxx it's actually an all-right game.  The randomness results in wildly varied board state from turn to turn, forcing one to make interesting tactical choices instead of just sticking to a strategy.  As a downside, it *is* quite luck-dependent, but less than one would initially believe.  The downside is worth the replayability.

RATING: 8
Neat card game with interesting mechanics and lots of player interaction. Heavy gamers may say that there is too much luck for their enjoyment, but what is actually going on is lots of potential for micro-managing your strategy. This game won't reward strategic players who like to plan 4 moves in advance, as you simply can't and shouldn't do this. Instead, I feel this game rewards those who remain flexible, play in the moment, and are capable of adapting to current game conditions.
This game allows for strong offensive cards that yield seemingly ridiculously high points, which can be countered by the right defensive card, if and when played correctly. Lots of "feast or famine" gameplay, but in they end it all balances out. Not my favorite game, but interesting enough I'll play it almost anytime.

*update: raised rating to an 8 after several more enjoyable 2 player games with my wife, and an awesome 4 player team game! Not many games offer team play opportunities, and fewer are actually fun. This game does both well. Not to mention that it is one of the best 2 player games I own, and the wife loves it, which is a huge plus!

In a 4 players game, just won the only one who was not motivated and stuck at 100% in the game, this is the main reason that forced me to sell it. This is a reliable proof that the game rewards the cards/combos random and not the players' skills/abilities. Sold to Amarillo114. From the first two card games published by Homoludicus as Iello partner in this format (box with magnetic lock) the good one, IMHO, was Biblios.

RATING: 7.8
I have some issues with it. The game seems horribly unbalanced with two players, with one person pulling ahead early and keeping the lead based on a lucky starting hand. I've also grown to dislike the text-heavy nature of it, because it takes more time than you have available on your turn to analyse what everyone is capable of and build a strategy. A lot of who does well is luck-based: pulling cards with the right symbols, or with dogmas that combo off each other, or other people not pulling the symbols that you need to control.

It's not a bad game, but every time I play it it feels like I have very little control over my own success or failure - picking the best card you happen to have picked up is not difficult to do, but it's also not overly interesting. You'll be faced with maybe one or two genuine choices that will benefit you and you can safely ignore the others.

RATING: 10
Absolutely incredible game in a small package. Plays 2 people great but goes up to 4 for pure chaos. It's a game that borrows heavily from Sid Meier's Civilization, but this is no Civ game; it's a pure technological advance game with the great technologies of humankind from the Stone Age up to the Digital Age. 

Lots of interaction, countering, hostile takeover and strategy here, with a healthy dose of luck and you can never count anyone out because one draw could complete a combo that swings the game to his victory. 

It's one of those perfect card games.

RATING: 5.5
Intriguing and overwhelming at the same time. I played casually, not bothering to read which of the 100+ unique cards my opponents were threatening me with and waiting for them to announce how they were hitting me, which actually worked out just fine. The game would probably be at least as fun after gaining familiarity of the cards and planning more strategically, though mixing players at the different levels would not produce a good experience.

I feel the designer is reacting to complaints about Glory to Rome, whether his own or his detractors', and I'm intrigued by the concept though I don't share the motivation. At first glance I worry it has a worse runaway leader problem and a different kind of learning curve (the explosion of card effects and their combinations is both larger and more impactful than that of the buildings in GtR, even if the basic action mechanisms are fewer and simpler) so it is more likely to be a stylistic difference for the audience. I'd like to try it again.

After half a dozen plays, I'm agreeing with the common complaint that this game is too chaotic and lucky. At times chaos is fun. At times it just feels punishing. I always seem to splay last. I'd really like to get in some two-player games to verify if the strategy goes up, as reported.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
Played once with four players (with the "team" variant where the played as two teams of two).  

In this game players built up a tableau of cards in front of them, with a goal of either scoring points or getting achievements (which often take points to get).  Cards have both symbols on them and special power text; when activating the special power text either all players with fewer symbols than you carry out the text, or all players with an equal or higher number than you carry out the text.  While this is a really cool mechanic (it means that if you have a special power that helps you, but someone else has as many symbols as you, then both of you carry it out), it leads to _insane_ complexities in a four player game.  We often would sit there for minutes looking over all five of our special powers, counting up everyone's symbols, and then planning moves accordingly.  And since the symbol counts change with every played card, things change rapidly (meaning planning ahead is tough).

To draw cards you pick from one of ten numbered piles, and can only draw cards from piles you've played in your tableau.  If there are no cards in the pile you want to draw from, then you draw from the next higher pile. This is another clever technique, as it slowly progresses the game to more advanced cards.  And, since scoring is based on the same "draw" mechanic, it means that if you are supposed to score "2", but there aren't any 2's, 3's, or 4's, then you score a "5" instead.

However, the game is, well, broken in my view.  Each card is unique, and has often-complicated special power text on it "I demand that you give me this card, then you can take a card from me, then I have to get you a soda, and you need to give me X-3/24 victory points".  While this excites special-power-card players who want to build the perfect combo, it just led to a tremendous amount of analysis-paralysis.  

Our game took close to two hours, and only ended because my team assembled a near-broken combo using the over-powered "Anatomy" card (wherein I could force our opponents to discard all of their points as well as cards from their tableau, holding them in perfect check while my opponent scored points without opposition). 

Overall I find this game entirely uninteresting; the special powers lead to incredible chaos during gameplay, and seem likely to lead to game-ends like we had (where one person blows the rest out of the water).

RATING: 10
First impression is an 8, as good or maybe better than Glory to Rome. We'll see how it holds up.

Innovation is the better 2-player game, Glory to Rome is better for 3-4.

Innovation is wonderful, full of broken cards and combos. Those bothered by large swings in fortune and lots of aggression need not apply. My wife and I LOVE Innovation as a 2-player game.

Regarding Glory to Rome vs Innovation:

It's hard to put into words. I feel like Innovation is a little snappier, a little faster-moving without sacrificing any of the interesting bits. The game state is a little more fragile and volatile as well. The technologies in Innovation are far less permanent than they are in GtR, so it rewards adaptation and thinking on one's feet a little bit more. GtR has more of the slow, lay-a-building-down-then-try-to-get-the-materials-into-it play.

Maybe Innovation just appeals to the impatient gamer in me. I feel like I get into a rhythm with Innovation that I never get with Glory to Rome, so maybe I just need more experience with the latter. I prefer the faster tempo of Innovation. 

Looking back at my logged plays I see that not a single month has gone by without at least one play of Innovation since I bought it in August of 2010. That's pretty telling.

RATING: 10
After playing a dozen two player sessions, I am delighted with this refreshing, clever, and unique card game. I don't think it's as chaotic as it's critics suggest, nor are the cards very difficult to learn. Once you gain familiarity with the cards, you have a good understanding of the flow of the game. As many other reviewers have pointed out, there are innumerable combinations that play out differently depending on the sequence and order of the tableaux. 

Innovation is about emerging properties and unfolding layers of consequences. This is one of the most interesting new games I have played in a long time. There's alot to think about. It's intense. But I also find it relaxing and very enjoyable. Great replayability and a whole lot of fun. 

On further plays, I realize that Innovation rewards improvisational and creative thinking. Although there are surely analytics involved, so often a player will come up with some clever solution to a seemingly insurmountable mess. Some people find order in chaos!

RATING: 6
Ten stacks of cards in a circle represent the different ages of civilization, from antiquity through the modern era. Players will acquire cards and slowly work up the ladder of these decks, trying to outwit their opponents with clever cardplay. The overlaying and "splaying" of cards creates really dynamic scenarios, but powerful cards can also cause one player to leapfrog ahead of the others. I suspect experience could help mitigate this possibility, but it can be frustrating for newer players. Still, it's a smart, mindbending game.

RATING: 7
Kaufdatum: 21.03.2011
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The more I play this the more I like it. It takes a lot of experience to learn how to handle the wild swings and to realize which cards are good for what. I'm still mediocre at it but find it an adventure every time. Haven't yet played with expansions but look forward to this.

Main things I've gleaned so far:
1) scoring early is a minor concern: the major tasks are getting all 5 colours down, building stacks, and splaying. Code of Laws and Sailing are ace.
2) tech up as quickly as you can (but don't cover up useful cards too soon)--Mathematics is particularly handy for this--and keep an eye for the attacks you're vulnerable: stealing from your hand, your score pile or your board? Plan accordingly.
3) lightbulbs, leaves and crowns are valuable in all ages; towers are nasty early on but a huge liability later.
4) try to snag a few achievements for individual ages or the 5 special achievements, to avoid losing early on, but also try to tech up efficiently to ages 8-10 so you have access to the big game-ending cards before opponents do, and then use them to shut the game down quickly or (if you're ahead in the score pile) try to draw an 11.

As with Tash-Kalar or Blue Moon, I love the individuality of the cards: you grow to anticipate each one coming up, and learn the ways they interact.

RATING: 6
Innovation reminds about Glory to Rome with its innovative use of playing cards. The cards in this game do not only have different functions such as abilities, colors, symbols and score but they can also be organized so that underlying cards contribute to your strength in different ways. Like in most card games, it's an advantage to know the many different cards and how they can be combined and the weak theme and extensive use of small text don't help. It's also difficult to follow a strategy since you're almost completely dependent on which cards you draw (and which your opponents let you keep). Nevertheless, Innovation may offer a lot of tactical highlights in the choice of card function as well as interaction in the struggle for score cards and majorities.

RATING: 8
In this civilization themed card game, one either draws cards, plays them on the table, or uses the special effect of the card. Each card has a number of symbols on it, so when you use the effect of the card, your opponents can also use the ability if they have at least the same number of symbols as you. Some effects are "demand" type effects which usually take away something from your opponents, but again, if you have at least the same symbols you are immune to the effect. Other effects allow you to "splay" your cards, so that even though cards on top of another, you can overlap symbols to give you more power.

The cards are ordered from level 1 to 10, with the more powerful cards at the higher levels. You start slowly at level 1 and work your way upwards. You win by getting a number of achievement cards which you pay for with scored cards. There are other sudden death win conditions as well.

Yes, there could be very destabilizing cards, but that is part of the game, and it's fun, and should play quickly with 2-3 players. With 4 players, I recommend the team game, otherwise it may be a long game. I'm enjoying this game, and looking forward to more plays, since I am only just scratching the surface. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Proud participant in the great Innovation Assembly Party of 2010.

I think it's a mistake to call this a civilization-building game.  That labels carries strong connotations that aren't on Innovation's agenda;  this is a take-that card game with a technology tree theme.  The "Fluxx for gamers" label is more accurate, but far less appealing.

Anyway, this is a chaotic game, but not in a way that I find irritating or highly prone to long streaks of bad luck.  Tons and tons of bizarre and interesting things can and will happen, and the replay value is immense.  Innovation occupies a prestigious spot in my game collection.

RATING: 10
It's incredible how much fun this game is considering its near bare-bones approach in art/graphic design and that it is basically a card game with 100 cards.

Once you understand the most-likely foreign playing terms of meld, tuck, splay and achieve, you'll be able to analyze every single card on your own, and gauge its immediate significance.  Now, to know what every card can do for you in the long term, well, that requires many playing sessions.

The progression from Age 1 cards with simple effects to Age 10 cards with crazy effects is a pleasant experience, as you feel your abstract civilization is growing and becoming more powerful, while giving you plenty more options to consider for your measly 2-actions-per-turn limit.  

The beautiful part comes in the combos that cards from all ages can potentially make.  You might think that a lowly Age card is useless, but if it is paired with the right card, it could make the opponent hurt in a most enjoyable way.  

Just as surprising, my girlfriend really got into it mid-way through our first game, and then asked to play it the very next day.  It is definitely a great 2-player game, which based on what everyone else has said, it should be its standard playing limit.  I can see how chaotic it would play with more than 2, plus there are a lot of tactical choices to consider on a player's turn, that with more than 2, it would bog down and wouldn't emphasize the flow of "if-you-play-that-I'll-counter-with-this".

Definitely try it out.  For a while I was thinking of getting the international edition with pretty pictures and such, but it is really inconsequential on the enjoyment you'll derive from its gameplay. Plus, the international edition has changed the terms and even some cards.

Update 1:  When the Deluxe Edition kickstarter popped up, there was no way in hell I wouldn't signed up for it.  Eagerly awaiting for its arrival in summer '16.

Update 2: Still waiting for Deluxe version but I got the old versions ready to be played at any time.
