RATING: 1
GF gave me this because I'm a big fan of Carl Chudyk's Glory to Rome and it was recommended by well known reviewers having opinions we used to care about. First letdown: no theme whatsoever and new "splay" mechanic is a total fluke - cards have more symbols at the bottom than at either side so you'll always splay them south. Compare to GtR in which anyone has a chance to put great cards into play since when you draw cards, you draw to your full hand size, here, you just draw one, hope and loose half a turn for it. In GtR, unbalance of cards is elegantly countered by the time you gotta spend to build them and the influence players have on available materials; in Innovation, cards need no time nor materials to be abused. In GtR, no card is better than a good combo; in Innovation, you've got despicably abusive single card loopholes. So while GtR is thematic (walls protect, forge gives materials etc.), tactical (multi-usage cards) and strategic (combo string and action selection), Innovation is the regress to utterly luck driven, meaningless piece of unfulfilling garbage with the worst case of luck swings ever seen.

[i]Cherry on top: the game's core is all about majorities yet there's nothing in the box to mark their fluctuations - pretty easy to tell when there's 4 cards on the table, a lil' less in mid game when everyone has 15 cards of 3 symbols among 6 different possible shapes in front of them.[/i]

RATING: 9
In 5 solo (Left hand vs. right) games, I've seen Fission fail, Electricity succeed, Coal blast through points before Age 3 was even complete, and Fermenting eat through the deck.  

Some people might call this chaotic.  I call it tactical.  With chaos, I feel out of control with either zero options or none that will make any difference.  With tactics, I feel that I can sway the playing field in my favor through good decisions.

This game is making me refine what I mean by thematic.  It obviously does not have bits and pieces that represent nations, territory, armies, etc.  The cards have no cool pictures of the technologies they represent.  But the more I play, the more I am amazed how thematically the cards use the board.  Sailing, an early achievement, would seem to help a nation diversify its' knowledge quickly.  However, after a short while, its' usefulness as a primary driver of new ideas is superseded by other, more useful concepts.  In one game, I felt the overwhelming rush of the current era (10), where it seems impossible to keep up with the changes going on.  I sensed how the incremental discoveries of ideas got faster and pushed the game toward a frenzied end.

Why this is not a 10:
+ I can see myself not always wanting to play
+ It has a constantly tactical nature, which would get tiresome after [long] awhile - I want just a touch more strategy.

RATING: 8
Interesting card-combo/tableau-building game with lots of different effects. I'm partial to the civ theme, and the progression through the ages provides a strong narrative arc, although the connection between the card names and effects is weak at best.

There is a fine balance to be tread between achieving icon superiority, tech-ing up and scoring; I tend to prioritise strongly in that order, often to my detriment.

I find it difficult to formulate any sort of coherent strategy, given the inability to control the cards drawn; I suspect this game is far more tactical.

The zero amount of effort on the card artwork jars; they could have tried to put some illustrations on the cards at least! As it is, it looks like a prototype.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Since all the cards have fairly complicated actions, I don't think that there is much likelihood that this game could have much strategic planning. Basically, you just look at what you have and try to make some points out of it. I wasn't really paying all that much attention to what other people had since it could change so easily.

After a second play, I think this game is too chaotic. I was effectively taken out of the game after a round or two since my cards kept getting stolen. Once you're behind in this one, there is virtually no way to make up any lost ground. If you're weak, you are going to be targeted by other cards, which will only serve to keep you down. If people are more equal, then there is more uncertainty about who will win, but there is much back and forth in this one, I wouldn't say that it's about planning. If you get a card that has a power that works with your setup, then you will do well, otherwise, you're going to fall behind.

RATING: 9
Apart from Hansa Teutonica, Innovation is the most exciting game I've played this year. It's simple and fast, yet deep enough in some of the tactical possibilities to be pleasing. As I mentioned previously: High-glass débutante Race for the Galaxy got crazy drunk one night and woke up in bed with both Fluxx AND Glory to Rome. Nine months later little Innovation was born. It will take a paternity test to find out which one is the real father...

Because of the fixed number of cards and achievements, gameplay varies wildly based on the number of players. With 2 it is strategic and you progress relatively slowly. With 4, progression is rapid, scoring can be dramatic, and the game can end very quickly.

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]I have owned both the Iello and Asmadi version of this game and have traded them both away. [COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]- Plays well with 2.
- There are a lot of cards in this game which leads to an unbelievable amount of potential interactions between cards.
- The Fission card: Wow; this went off in one of our games and it was crazy, it basically resets the game and destroys everything you have done.
- However; I find the game a bit too random and swingy.
- You don't always feel like it was your skill that caused you to win; instead you got lucky with a few card draws.
- I also found that the game usually ends way before you get to the higher ages (8 - 10) which means you very rarely see very many of those cards.[/i]

規則:
*將1~10時代牌照牌背分類洗勻，每種再拿一張放到中央當作成就卡，旁邊放五張特殊成就卡
*每人從時代1抽兩張，一張打出，字母靠前的順位先，2人時首家獲是四人時首兩家只有一個行動
*玩家輪流行動，每次有兩個行動點，每個行動可選
>抽一張牌 根據你目前場上牌的最高數字，抽對應數字的牌，如果該牌沒有往下抽，如果等級較低的都沒有就抽目前最低的
>出一張牌  蓋在同色牌上方，如果該色已經展開則照展開的方式放置
>啟動場上一張牌的效果  所有場上對應符號跟你一樣多的玩家都強迫做，或是不能成為目標，順序為你的左手玩家先開始，你尾家，只要有一個玩家做你就之後抽一張牌
>拿成就  面前的頂牌要有一張比目標成就卡高，且分數要是目標成就卡的五倍才能拿，拿到時放在自己個人圖板右邊，不需要支付分數
*特殊成就卡一達成條間就免費拿，同時達成就從行動玩家順時針順位先拿
*遊戲在2/3/4人時先拿到6/5/4成就的贏，或是當要抽超過時代10的卡時結束，分數高獲勝

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An interesting game of collecting and placing cards to your tableau, looking for powers that complement each other and striving to gain majorities in the various symbols that drive the powers so as to allow you to execute them without people riding on your coat-tails Puerto Rico style. Your normal actions are to draw a card, play a card or execute a power, and you get two actions. The card powers in the lower decks are weak (you collectively start with deck 1 and work up to deck 10) but each higher deck has more and more powerful powers, becoming quite dramatic in the top decks. Powers range from better card drawing, being able to spread cards so symbols underneath count towards your majorities, to stealing cards from other players and, of course, scoring. You can only have 1 active power in each colour, so there are continual decisions on foregoing a useful power to get a different one. Another is on whether to just concentrate on building symbol majorities now and worry about scoring later, or go for the quick scoring grabs now to minimise the required effort later. If you want to do one of your powers when you don't have the majority in the particular symbol it requires, you can still do it, but everyone with equal or more in that symbol can also do it, so that can be an interesting decision at times. Lots of cards and powers disappear through the game, so games range from highly interactive with lots of pussage to quiet scoring engine type games, and anywhere in between. And like the Melbourne weather, potentially all in one game. I found my initial plays entertaining and it was interesting to see how it unravels differently each time. There's potential for rich-get-richer and player carnage, but I don't mind that in a card-power game that's over in a decent timeframe. One of the other attractions (to me, but can be considered turnoffs to many) is the ebb and flow in scoring power through the game and the possibility of huge come-from-behind wins - there's always hope once you move into the powerful cards of the final decks.

RATING: 5
What I like:
- Portable
- Pretty quick to teach and play (60 minute 4p learning game)
- The attacks are mostly non-targeted

What I don't like:
- Not a fan of "special ability" and combo card games
- Seemed like some "rich get richer" aspects (although also with a few catchup mechanisms)
- It's frustrating when your best play also happens to benefit the leader
- The theme is even thinner than most light civ games

Playing this the first time was very disorienting. You have no idea what kinds of cards are even in the game. Many of the card abilities are tricky, and require very careful reading to get right. 

It's an odd mix of high randomness (what cards you get) combined with skill needed to actually recognize and execute the right combinations. Some bash-the-leader seems expected/required.

RATING: 6.5
Based on one play: A very interesting mechanic. You play cards in order to move up in levels and have to get achievements to win. However, the innovations you place in front of you all have powers and you need to have more of a symbol then others to be able to use the powers without others also using it. Based on what you draw, you hope that your cards will remain so that you can build a combo, but there is a lot of back and forth between players, so from one turn to another things can change and you need to rethink your strategy (if you can have one at all). Interesting mechanic, but at the end, fairly luck based and dependent on whether you get targeted a lot by others.

RATING: 5
Really enjoying this 5 plays in. Like other card-based games (RftG, for example), it will take some more plays to learn the deck and become comfortable with the combos and strategies. So far, though, we've seen a wide range of approaches be successful, from an early scoring blitz to teching up quickly to one of the age 10 win-condition changers. 

I love the theme, and the selection of technologies is fantastic. There are quite a few non-obvious ones that, surprisingly for a non-war game, make you think about the history behind the game. "Canning" seems a little silly the first time you see it, but really, long-term preservation of healthy food is a big deal. 

I'm definitely inclined to think this game is at it's best with fewer players, as it becomes much more unstable with more.

RATING: 8
I really like this game, but I have found out a couple issues with actually playing it. First, some people despise it, because it seems a bit chaotic. Second, I had the misfortune of playing this with a guy who took his sweet time to think about every possible combination and read every card like twenty times. This is the kind of game where the flow is good once you know the cards pretty well. A good player will know which cards are played, which are left and plan his strategy accordingly. The game mechanics may also take a bit for people to take full advantage of. I would heavily suggest to those teaching the game to not go for a full game, and instead show specific instances of situations before hand. There is very little chance a first time player will win unless someone that knows the game actually gives the game up.
Finally, the game does require interaction, and you will be messing up other peoples games and vice-versa. This means some people may get mad at you when you keep pulling off the same crippling move over and over.
I really like the concept of the game, and the competitive aspect of it, but it is definitely not for everyone.

C’est probablement mon jeu de cartes 2 joueurs préféré. Les cartes sont magnifiques : elles ont de belles couleurs, des symboles bien trouvés et les différents domaines et civilisations laissent libre cours à l’imagination. 

Pour gagner, vous avez trois options : 
-	accumuler un maximum de points d’influence dès le début pour pouvoir acheter des périodes, 
-	opter pour la production de ressources qui permet aussi de débloquer des domaines, 
-	ou tenter de faire évoluer votre civilisation le plus rapidement possible pour débloquer des cartes plus élevées (à partir du niveau 8 les conditions des dogmes de certaines cartes peuvent vous permettre de gagner en quelques coups). 
En matière de gestion des ressources, certaines cartes permettent de décaler une pile de couleur dans plusieurs orientations : ce système ingénieux permet des évolutions rapides (jusque-là, je n’avais jamais vu ce système). 
Les novices pourront très vite se prendre au jeu puisqu’il suffit de mémoriser quelques règles de base pour pouvoir jouer, l’essentiel étant écrit sur les cartes. 
Si le jeu de base vous plait et que vous préférez les parties qui durent 2/3 heures, je recommande l’extension Innovation: Echoes of the Past qui permet d’optimiser le jeu en limitant le facteur chance.

RATING: 8
Innovation is an incredible game where you stack cards in colored piles. The idea is on your turn chose 2 actions, which for the most part will be draw a card, play a card or activate a card. When a card activates, if another player has equal or more matching symbols to you, they get the card effect as well, before you do. If the type of effect is attack-ish, then if they have more symbols they are immune to the effects. The game also has mechanisms by which you can spread your card piles out in different directions revealing the icons of cards played earlier in the game that were covered by better cards. The game is intense, with tons of player interaction and digging for cards to keep up with or counter your opponents. As the game progresses cards get more and more crazy, so the tension really ramps up.

There are so many cards with different effects in this game that the game can start to feel too random. For example, if your opponent has an effective attack card, it could be a few turns before you manage to draw an effective defense card, if you do at all. This can discourage people from wanting to play again, even though once seen, allowing yourself to be vulnerable to these cards becomes a calculated risk. 

The game also works poorly with more than 3 players, as if you let all players effect all players, the chaos gets more chaotic, and if you play with the rule that your effects only impact the players directly to your left and right, then when the opponent across from you starts to win, there's not much you can do.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:nostar: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Looks complicated.

Update:  ...but isn't really, once you get your head around the point of the game.  And I enjoyed this enormously when I got to that place.  
Innovation is a fantastic card game that feels like a Euro without the board, the wooden pieces, and the pretense.
Sure, the theme is somewhat abstracted, but the game itself is fast playing, brutal fun loaded with special powers on the cards (called "dogmas") that either benefit you, or help the entire table.  
I will say, however, that I can understand why opinions are divided on it. It can be a little nasty, and there is a runaway leader issue.  But I really, really like it.

RATING: 10
:thumbsup: Love, love, love this game! Innovation has been our most played game so far and the most rewarding. It's my poster-child for dynamic games because there is so much to consider with each hand of cards, the game state is constantly changing, and the possibilities are numerous every turn. It happened to be a style of game that clicked with me immediately. For those who lean towards well-developed strategies, it might be a hard game to switch gears for as it will only punish attempts at long-term thinking. For a gut player, it comes naturally. After repeated play, my husband and I have seen that it is possible to mitigate the chaos of the game. It becomes a fine balance between short-term advantages and knowing when to ditch them for something new. The decisions are tough as you are faced with the cost/benefit analysis of brutalizing your opponent vs actions that are more directly beneficial to your Score Pile or stash of Achievements (sometimes they coincide :p), all while keeping a constant eye on your opponent's cards. The level of tension that mounts as the game progresses into the higher Ages is palpable when you know the potentially explosive cards that await. The majority of our games have ended extremely close and even losses can feel amazing after so many critical decisions.  

:thumbsdown: I own the Asmadi version and it's not much to look at. Thematically, some find the theme pasted on but I find the mechanics of the game highly thematic as you learn the cards and see how the actions reflect the technology  named. However, in early plays it's harder to be mindful of those details. At this point, I'm partial to the Asmadi version. There is so much going on action-wise, I think I would find more graphics to be busy and distracting. Charges of randomness are valid as well. Every once in a while we have a stinker of a game where one of us just has way too many powerful cards early on and despite having so many possibilities for actions, the other player just can't catch up. It's going to happen but it happens less and less with more experience. Overall, this aspect does not diminish my enjoyment of the game. 

:star::star::star:Player interaction
:star::star::star:High tension
:star::star::star:Interesting decisions
:star::star::star:Dynamic game play*
:star::nostar::nostar:Strong narrative
:star::star::star:Desire to replay/enjoyment
:star::star::star:Re-playability

RATING: 7
This game is absolutely insane. Other games that are described as 'chaotic' feel like a tea party compared to this. Whatever cards are on the board at any time will likely be gone or buried within five minutes; this game is 99% short-term tactics with almost no long-term strategy. It's not that well balanced; you have to accept that sometimes one player is going to draw better cards and steamroll the other(s). But it will never happen the same way twice, and Innovation throws enough decisions at you that even if you're getting creamed you feel like there are still some cool things for you to do. Let that help you decide if this game is right for you.

RATING: N/A
This is a 2p-only game for me, and it maybe probably ranks as my favorite game. My neighbor and I play it nearly every time we get together to play cards, and we can play a game in about 30 minutes. 

One of my favorite features of any game is "on your turn, you may take two actions. These are the four actions; mix and match however you want." Boom, done. I'm really not big on Action Selection Restrictions (worker placement, roll dice to dictate actions, use a mancala to govern your actions, etc), and prefer more open games. I also like games where you can move backwards in order to move forwards, and that's a little harder to explain; there aren't many examples of those, but let me try and explain with Innovation. 

I love how I can pillage my own score pile, or blow up my tableau, or cover up a strong card to make a stronger play later, or lean on an action that puts me ahead in one area but buries me in another. I like that basically every card is broken, too. I know this game incites strong feelings, but this is one that has absolutely settled down the more I have played it. It doesn't feel nearly as uncontrollable as when I first started playing it. It makes me relax. Did my scorepile disappear in three turns. Not a problem. Relax. You've seen this before. Are you behind 4-1 on Achievements? Not a problem. Let's see if we can't get into the 8s soon. 

Even after close to 50 plays, we still see things each game we've never seen before, and we still chuckle as we discover new jokes about the theme of each card.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Innovation is a tricky game to rate.  It's become one of my favourite card games, alongside Race for The Galaxy and Dominion, yet I can't give it a 10 or even a 9.

The reason is that the game experience can be pretty variable.  Some games are very chaotic; others play out in a much more orderly - and tactical - fashion.  Sometimes there will be a runaway leader; other times, a long, drawn out slugging match.  Some players love the game, others actively hate it.

My view is that the experience can also depend to a large extent on the number of players; with two, the game is much tighter, requires a closer eye on what you can do and what your opponent is up to; add in two more players and the chaos is on a par with Fluxx - I still enjoy the game, but it's hard to argue with those that don't once we're at that level of luck versus skill.  My view is that three players is where the sweet spot lies.

One criticism that you could never make of the game is that it's not varied; every game seems to play out differently, a card that was all-powerful in one game is all but useless the next, a strategy might work with one set of cards and utterly fail with another.  And it's probably this level of variety that has kept my interest in the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I like that each card is different and unique.  I can imagine that with more plays and knowledge of the deck there would be considerable groaning amongst the players that such and such a player got a particular card.

If I were to rank Innovation in a hierarchy with Race for the Galaxy and Glory to Rome, I would say that Race > Innovation > Glory to Rome.  Both Innovation and Glory to Rome are slower than Race for the Galaxy because there are so many things to consider.  The only time there are a ton of considerations to make in Race is when you've got a full hand of cards.  It can be time consuming to decide the implications of spending each one.  In Innovation, so far, I've found it to be rare to have a hand of more than four cards.  This means that the decisions you have to make come from what's already on the table rather than what is in your hand.  I prefer the decision tree of possible decisions (from the cards in your hand) rather than the decision of which power you've already got to activate.   I find the potential of cards in hand much more interesting.

Additionally, Innovation frequently causes players to have no cards in hand, so if the players have no powers that they'd like to use on the table, they can pretty much draw one and play one.  It leaves me with a feeling of simplicity.  My only decision is to figure out if the random card I've just drawn is good enough or should I just try for random luck again.  I'm someone who enjoys Fluxx, so I don't always mind this sort of mindless non-decision making.  That being said, the most experience player won every game that I've played so far, so I know the game is certainly more than dumb luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Innovation is a very unique card game which resolves around very simple basic actions(play a card, activate a card, draw a card or dominate). As the game progresses each player has to deal with constantly changing situations and more complex structures that force the players to react to everything that happens on the table. Strategy comes from the fact that each player should know how they are going to win the game and how they are not going win the game. This requires players to ditch their previous approach and a create new strategy. Innovation is also very tactical game. Reacting to your opponents' moves is crucial part of the game. 

The gameplay has a narrative feel and each game tells you a different story even though the game is based on a very abstract system, the theme still manages to arise on top and playing the game has a highly thematic feel. 

Innovation is still a very polarizing game; I think most people will either hate or love Innovation and I think "the middle ground" between the fans and the people who hate this game is very narrow. The game has its swings and luck is part of the game but better player will most likely succeed. Definitely best as a 2-player game, works as a 3-player game to some degree but it's already pushing the limits. Don't play this game with four players! Overall, Innovation is a very good 2-player card game that I'm happy to have in my collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
20+ games in, and every game has played out differently. And almost every game has seen a tight finish. Yeah, it's a 10.

=======================================================

Updated rating after five plays. I'm starting to appreciate the dynamic push and pull of icons versus dogmas, scoring versus tech level. You really have to be flexible, pressing your advantage when you can, while setting up for long-term plays. The variability and breadth of play are astonishing.

======================================================

First game was a head-scratcher, but by the second we were cheerfully pulling levers and setting up combos. Lots to process at the table, and I wouldn't play it with more than three. But we all enjoyed the way the dogmas ramped up in effect, and the difficult choices between building your icon strength, playing dogmas, and scoring achievements.

And this is no dry exercise in solo engine-building. The interaction in Innovation is intense; sometimes brutual and sometimes extremely subtle.

Looks to be serious depth and replayability here. I could easily see the rating on this game rising if I play it frequently with experienced players.

RATING: 9
Among my favourite card games - probably only Dominion and Race for the Galaxy come ahead of it. What is brilliant about Innovation is that every single card can seem game-breakingly powerful in any individual game, but that that this applies to a different card in every game! I do think it suffers with more than 2 players - not so much because of the often-complained-about chaos factor (although this does apply more with more players), but more because the piles empty quicker so that everyone tends to advance through the ages at the same, fairly quick, rate. In 2-player games I find that it is a legitimate strategy - indeed, one of my favourites - to aim to advance through the ages quicker than your opponent and then either win with a late-game card or switch to scoring and achieving when you have a good "engine" for doing so. In 3 or 4-player, everyone else advances quickly anyway, so your best chance is just to score and achieve quicker than everyone else - this makes the game more luck-dependent, as you may not get the cards you need to do this.

With 2, though, this is one of the most replayable and most intriguing card games out there. Despite having only 2 actions each turn, the number of options you have is staggering (even more so if you are playing with one or both of the expansions). For a while my wife (who is the only person I can play 2p games against with any regularity) didn't like the game so much and didn't play it with me, but at around Christmas time in 2014 she suddenly got back into it - she actually bought me both expansions for Christmas, and since then we have had plenty of games with Echoes of the Past and a few with Figures as well. (I lose horribly almost every time, as is not uncommon when I game with her...)

RATING: 6
Innovation is a clever card game where the cards played in front of you represent technological advances that give you advantages in some respects. You can invoke the power of a technology (dogma) and the player with the most of the representative symbol takes advantage of said power benefiting himself or directly impacting other players. 

You really need to know all the cards well and which pair well together. I find it difficult to plan a long term strategy and often the more tactical player, not stuck on any one path will be successful. I'm always building a big infrastructure with grandiose plans while others are scoring and pushing the game to end quickly. Also, the higher player count, the more chaotic the game is. Quite good for 2-players though.

RATING: 7
(+) Variety abounds in this game, between the cards and combinations and various technological eras.  
(+) High player interaction, by virtue of the requirement of paying close attention to card symbols in play.  
(+) Excellent story arc, better than a most games. A clear ramping up of pace is fueled by 'innovation', and frankly, it's quite fun.

RATING: 9.5
I  can't say enough good things about Innovation. Having played the 2 player version a couple dozen times, I've seen the game play out in a variety of ways. In my last play, I started out with a strong lead of 3 achievements. I then made some mistakes, and my opponent blew by me with 5 consecutive achievements before I could get one more. He ended up in the 10 deck while I was mulling around in the 5-6.  An amazing occurrence happened that forced him to draw and meld a card from the 10 deck, it just so happened that the card he melded yielded me the victory. 

There is a staggering amount of depth and variability hidden within, and I'm still being surprised by the game after 25+ plays. It's highly tactical, and knowledge if the cards helps a lot. The learning curve is pretty steep. In fact, I outright disliked Innovation after my first couple plays. After a while, I started thinking about my previous plays, and all the ways I could improve upon my strategy. I took a leap, purchased the game, and the rest is history. Innovation is probably the only game where my opinion went from intense dislike to avid appreciation. Makes me wonder what other gem are out there that I wrote off too quickly.

RATING: 9

It's usually chaotic, there's quite a few swings, there's quite a bit of direct interaction, it's tactical over strategic etc. At first I even though the theme worked. And the art... It seems to me the general sentiment is that the Asmadi version is ugly, but far from it. It just emphasises clarity, a necessity for this game. The iello version changes the terminology so that it's more confusing, not less, and the icons are harder to identify requiring you to lean over to see them instead of just glimpsing across the table. Definitely recommend the Asmadi version.

Overall it's simple to explain but your first play will take considerably longer than the estimated playing time. The complexity is in the card abilities and how they mesh. I love how even Age 1 cards can be just the best thing to have on top in the later ages. The potential swings sort of quarantee you're never out, but sometimes it's just impossible to make them happen. Rating has gone down from 9 to 8 because the game's all mechanics and it's a little too hard to fight against bad card draws a little too often. I wouldn't mind getting screwed by luck were there something else to enjoy in the game, like a developing story or character or battles between others.

2014: I came back to this and the rating went back up to 9 after a game where I won on special achievements as the underdog. I guess we've learned to fight bad card draw.

RATING: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Visual Appeal: (3)
The box design is pretty spartan. Not a problem for me because all it has to do is hold those precious cards! Some people might have a problem with the cards' appearance but I think they are just right. Typical of Chudyk games, they show a lot of information efficiently in a small space. As the game progresses with splaying tableaux, it is an aesthetically pleasing card game to look at.

Components: (4)
The cards are of decent quality. Each expansion has a different color back and the print quality has been consistent throughout. I have been unable to sleeve them because the backs need to be visible and I hate penny sleeves. Luckily the cards are holding up great even after many dozens of plays. [I look forward to grabbing the deluxe edition when it finally becomes available!]

Theme: (5)
I know many people will disagree, but I happen to think that this game incorporates theme in a clever way! There's an almost tongue-in-cheek abstraction of the "innovation" that each card represents. My favorite example is the Pirate Code card which allows you to "pillage" an opponent's board and "loot" their score pile. Although it states the action in more dry mechanical terms, I think the flavor is still in there. Not every card has a clear connection to the title, but if you stretch the imagination I think you can make it work. I always have a giggle when I think of a new one. 

Learning: (3)
This game takes 10-15 minutes to teach, and the rest is picked up during play. It can be frustrating to play against a more experienced player because knowledge of the cards is key. Learning Innovation is an ongoing task for me as I've reached the point where I want to learn strategies to counter specific cards. 

Gameplay: (5)
There are some really great screw you cards in this game. I play mostly with 2 players. Once you know what to look for, it can be a real battle of wits to keep your symbols in perfect position to attack, defend, and advance in the right balance. Stealing points from your opponents feels so satisfying. Races for the achievements can get very close and there is a lot of tension surrounding who can get the first 3 the fastest. The game in terms of card abilities is very tactical: what dogma ability would really help me right now? In terms of building a board and splaying for symbols, the strategic element of the game begins to show itself.

Overall Fun: (5)
I have played this game many many times with all of the released expansions and it just does not wear out for me. I like figuring out new card combinations to add to my arsenal of tricks, and the player interactions are a good match for what I want in a 2 player game. 3+ player games can run a little slow and that was the main complaint that I heard when I played with a larger group of friends. "Isn't this a bit long for a card game?" I suppose that is true. Currently my favorite 2p game and I'm not itching to dethrone it anytime soon.

RATING: 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This game is based around a very simple concept: splaying. The idea is that when cards are stacked in a pile, you can lay them out to reveal parts of the cards below them. Since cards have one icon on the right side of the card, two on the left, and three on the bottom, splayed card piles progress naturally: left to right to up.

Fortunately obtaining icons doesn't win you the game on its own (unless you happen to get cards that say stuff like "win the game if you have twenty lightbulb icons".) This is great because the game doesn't milk this one mechanic too heavily. Instead, you are focusing on obtaining individual cards - some with powerful actions for advancing your board and others which increase your score. This means you need to continually balance your board to try to accomplish many things at once.

I used to have this game at an 8 because I felt it was unbalanced and just amounted to crazy fun - a fully realized Fluxx, basically. However (in part due to Isotropic play), I've realized that this game is only as good as your opponents. Once you get fifty games under your belt, you begin to see that, well, this game has it all. Nothing beats this for me. It is strategic, it is tense, it is a fight-for-your-life game. It hits me harder than Dominion because it operates at such a quick pace. This game hits my adrenaline. What kind of game does that?

Carl Chudyk did well with Glory to Rome, but it has a precedent in other role selection games (RftG, San Juan, etc.) However, Innovation is truly unique. Nothing feels like it.

Lastly, the interaction in this game is unbelievable. You are fighting over cards in hand. You steal from each other's boards and score piles. You demand and share and race. And it's not the back-stabbing, subterfuge sort of interaction. It is a battle.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Dominate a majority of the 10 ages. Tried and true 2 actions per turn, used to draw, play, score (dominate), or very often in the mid-game and after, activate the cards you already have on the table. Activating is beneficial when you have more of the right resources on the table, to either be able to activate an attack or to prevent leeching, though sometimes you do want to let your opponent leech if it doesn't help as much (plus you get a card out of it) or actually hurts him. Visual aplomb is achieved by splaying cards in different directions to achieve cumulative effect of all card icons in a color stack.

Race for influence and ensure you have the spare actions to stake your dominance (else your opponent might). Wasting an action to score is a nice catch-up mechanism, similar to that in Twilight Struggle.

Very CCG-like in terms of keeping all the abilities on the table straight in your head.

Surprisingly thematic, despite needing to game some abilities (I draw after you with this ability, so I get a better card, yay). Splaying in each direction appears to have a concept attached to it. And lots of nicely implemented ideas like Statistics (hah!) and Coal result in drastic externalities on your civilization when you throttle them, providing a nice commentary on over-use.

Completes the Chudyk combo: Innovation for 2er, Glory to Rome for higher player counts. Playing with more than 2 would result in too much chaos and bean-counting. I don't like that you really need to memorize the cards in each Age in order to do well though (like 7 Wonders).

4+4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Played once with 3P. Although there are a few superficial similarities to Glory to Rome, Innovation is quite a different beast altogether. For starters, almost nothing in Innovation is safe – Influence can be stolen and inventions destroyed. Granted, there are a few strategic decisions such as deciding whether to splay your cards, but Innovation is very much a tactical game where one has to observe what one's opponents are doing. 

This probably means that Innovation plays better with fewer people as the chaos can be reined in a little, and there are fewer icons to keep track of. My first game took a little long as there is plenty of text to read, and conditions of the game can change quite abruptly, although I think familiarity with the cards would allow players to better anticipate the kind of hijinks which can ensue.

Completely unexpected, odd, bizarre, nerve-wrecking, hair-greying bit of a brilliance.

RATING: 9
Like my girlfriend says, this game is "Jazzy." Possible that it becomes more static at an expert level, but as far as I can tell this game encompasses improvisation better than any game out there.

Definitely best with 2 players, for many reasons, but most of all that most of the beauty in this game comes from dancing and maneuvering around an opponent, matching him on some icons and exploiting him on others. Slowing or speeding up the tempo based on who has the momentum. Having even one more opponent would make that pretty much impossible.

The cards somehow seem very balanced and well thought out... despite seeming extrmely broken. You just have to stand back in awe at the design. So brilliant. And when you realize that the cards actually have thematic connotations as well? This is just an amazing game through and through. I enjoy it a lot, but I think I admire it even more than I enjoy it.

Also, a quick read through of the short rulebook will leave you assuming that this game is gonna have some serious runaway leader problems, but it [i]doesn't[/i]. Somehow, the most killer combos become outdated and archaic. Again, this has to be the most subtly thematic game out there. Probably one of the top 5 games I've ever played, from a "design" standpoint.

(5 Design + 4 Enjoyment)

RATING: 3
I've come up with a shorter variant of the game that captures gameplay in its essence.  

In a three player game, randomly deal out a 10 card, an 8 card and a 4 card.  The person with the 4 card gets to trade for the 10 card and wins. The old 10 card guy comes in last even though he had the high number because ultimately he drew the wrong card and someone else drew the right one.

In a four player game, randomly deal out a 10 card, an 8 card, a 4 card and a 1 card. The person with the 8 card gets to trade with the 10 to win, the 4 keeps their card it's useless, and all three take the 1 card away from the last player so that he can't actually play.

RATING: 10
Chaotic at first, Innovation is a card game with lots of interaction, some of it helpful and some of it destructive. If you hope to win, you can't ever ignore what your opponents are doing. Because of this, two players is best. With lots of powerful cards, and even more powerful combos, there are countless routes to victory. At the same time, those routes aren't so unpredictable that planning becomes impossible. Every game is different, so you have to adapt. 

It's hard to overstate the genius of the design. What looks and plays like an abstract card game never stops surprising thematically. Not only do card functions reflect their names, they combo together as you'd expect. For example, one of the best early game combos is The Wheel and Domestication. Another powerful early card, Sailing, remains useful even into later ages. I've used it to transition into Colonialism, which in turn proved useful for several ages before petering out. As you advance through the ages, cards become more destabilizing. Some, like AI, are game-changing. There are Easter eggs everywhere. Finding them is as much fun as winning, and the one often leads to the other.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
A civ-themed game, apparently, although that is completely lost in the actual gameplay where you are actually looking more at all the funny little symbols than at the names of the cards. There are 10 decks of cards, arranged from early historic times forwards. Players start with 2 cards in hand, deciding to keep 1 in hand and meld the other. On your turn, you get just 2 actions. These could be to simply draw a card (level equal to highest level top card in your display), play a card (from your hand, on top of matching colored stack in your display -- there are five colors in game), take an achievement (based on cards in your "score" pile; first player to earn 8-n achievements wins), or take an action on one of your top cards. The last -- card actions -- is the most interesting. 

The cards have several symbols on them. Card actions key off these symbols: the "I demand" actions attack people with fewer matching symbols showing. The other actions are taken first by people with the same or higher number showing, then you get to go. Actions do a variety of things, eg add cards to your score pile, attack other players display/hand/score pile, splay cards (up, left or right; results in having more symbols showing, and may help earn achievements), etc. 

Once one player gets behind, though, it seems pretty difficult to catch up. You only draw, at most, two cards (barring special card actions) while others can continue attacking you.

It is still kind of interesting and entertaining watching the game unfold, despite simultaneously feeling that it is all almost completely random and based on the cards you happened to draw. I would probably play it again, but I won't seek it out.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

I love the theme of improving technology through different ages and the variety of options on the 100+ cards. The "splaying" mechanic is a neat and original concept as well.

There is a lot of chaos in this game, you can only be so careful and strategic till an opponent or even you yourself mistakenly bring the whole thing crashing down and you're forced to change direction.  This is something many people may not enjoy.  I love it!

Best with 2 players
Recommended with 2, 3, 4 players

What do you do with a problem like Innovation? It has clever and novel mechanics as you manage your board, and it has a number of different viable tactics for play. The race to achieve before your opponents starts tense and only gets worse as the game goes on.

However, there's a few inherent problems that Innovation doesn't quite solve. Each of the 100+ cards has a mountain of text and rules associated with it. The rules are usually simple to understand, but the number of cards combined with how often the table changes means that you often need to read every card on the table before you can make an effective decision. I suspect that doctors may use a game of Innovation as the clinical diagnosis for analysis paralysis.

Innovation also suffers from the sheer randomness of the game. Each card is unique, and you're often drawing randomly from the same pile of cards as your opponents. Unless you're playing a strategy of being the first to a new age to find particular cards, you're at the mercy of what you draw or are forced to play blind in many cases. As such, coherent strategies are impossible for more than 2-3 turns before the state of the board has changed dramatically.

I really enjoy aspects of Innovation and I'll never say no to playing. However, I never feel like I earned my victories or defeats. That's fine in a rules-light game that's more about the experience than the competition (hi Galaxy Trucker!), but a friendly game Innovation is not.

The online version makes reading the cards of your opponents much easier and makes it a better game to play, oddly enough.

I should really start posting these reviews somewhere else.

Currently I'm still feeling (1) and wouldn't mind playing it a few more times to see if there's a game in there I enjoy. I certainly *want* to enjoy it at this point -- and that's a very good sign.

RATING: 6
I'm often a bit lukewarm on games of this ilk, where you have to build up a little economy/civ to attack other players, defend yourself and achieve objectives. My criteria for liking or disliking such games probably differs from most other people - I don't mind a bit of randomness or imbalance; what I dislike is needing to know what cards other players have but having trouble remembering or seeing them. For me, Innovation is a big hit: interesting mechanisms, plenty of options, clear iconography, relatively fast play. Very interactive - many of your powers allow other players to benefit or suffer depending on how they've responded to your cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Extraordinarily strategic and diverse card game. The premise is to collect influence from cards across 10 ages (from the First Ages to the Digital Age), with each age increasing in value. This in turn allows a player to dominate an age and victory is secured once a certain number of ages have been dominated depending on player numbers. The brilliance of 'Innovation' is found in the fact that players continually have to adapt to the game around them as cards change the conditions by which influence is accrued. This means that there is no 'engine' that a player has to find and that will work consistently. Due to the volume of cards in the game and the vast variety of combinations, one game differs wildly from the next and this ensures enormous replayability. This is a game that takes repeated plays for a player to become aware of how to approach it in terms of decision making, but together with Carl Chudyk's other great card game 'Glory to Rome', there are no better examples of this style of game that I know of.

RATING: 4
after one play. I find it rather clunky and the mechanics totally detached from it's theme. It seems quite long for what it is; a card game that seems to have a high dose of luck. Also the art is quite good, the icons are easy to use although one set of icons has no use at all (needed for the expansion which always frutrates me). 

It's clunky because I seemed to be always looking at the other guys cards and counting or asking him how many leaf icons or light bulb icons he has. It seems hard to from a clear strategy other than simply responding to whatever cards came up; more seat of your pants than long term planning.  Its detached from the theme because the long term strategies have to do with fanning your cards in certain directions as well as how you display the icons on the cards. I dont get what this has to do with the theme of a society and it's innovations. Only one play and with only two players so maybe this will get better with more experience. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Initially looking at Innovation without knowing how to play I thought the game looked bland. All brown and pastelly. But then I played it and liked it from the first play.

It seems like hyperbole to say that no two games of Innovation are the same, like saying no two games of Carcassonne are the same since the randomness of tile drawing and choices for tile placement mean the final board will always look at least subtley different in every game. In the end though Carcassonne is still Carcassonne with its limited decision making. Innovation on the other hand is more like playing Ace of Aces if it was a 1,000 page Choose-Your-Own-Adventure book and you got to have up to 5 pages open at any given time and were able to glance at several to dozens of other pages as well for a vast array of 'If/Then' possibilities. Sure, you've probably been on page 265 before, but not when you've also been on page 899, and certainly not when you opponent has been on page 6 and 28.

In the 10+ plays I've had (I don't think I've recorded them all) four of those games have ended due to, or with the threat of one particular card that allows the game to end with an early win for one player having 20 lightbulb icons (Empiricism). Even those four games played out dramatically different. Different certainly in getting to Age 8 to reveal that card and even different in it's affect on each game (Empiricism is 2 for 4 so far, not at all a sure thing, but nothing to ignore either).

Somehow this game is more than still enjoyable. It's still growing on me. I'm close to wanting to play it all the time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.6
Nice 2 players game. It's Glory-to-Rome-ish, with a lot of interaction and quite an amount of chaos, but it gets more strategic once you play more.

It's a short game, without a lot of setup time and usually short. Luck clearly plays a role in the game, but also does skill. The game punishes both not having a long-term strategy and stick too much with one of them. Since the game states changes very fast, you must always have a plan B. You can't defend for every attack you will be launched... so just try to adapt to the new situations.

The game length is quite unpredictable. Most of the games are quite short: one player gets a big advantage, gets the first 3 achievements and the game ends before the other one can fully recover. However, if the game reaches the higher eras without a clear leader, it can take a while. Sometimes, even with one player in a better position, it's really hard to translate the board domain into getting achievements, so the game takes quite a long time without being funny. Maybe this is because, since most of the games don't require age 8+ cards, we're still unfamiliar with the strategy of age 9 and 10 tableau. All in all, we've had some games whit 15 minutes of agony for one player which aren't fun at all.

All in all, it isn't the best game in the market, but it is still an original short game. It's fun for 2 people if you want something light.

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After 3 Plays: This game, while certainly not a true "strategy" game, has several redeeming and fun qualities about it. First of all, the idea of moving through the ages of technology is well captured and the theme is fun. The game mechanics all mesh quite well together and in ~innovative~ ways every game. While several posts have complained about the failure to include a "catch-the-leader" mechanic, we have had no problem with this and often the game has been radically changed in a single turn by a well played use of tactics. All this together yields a cohesive and creative game with interesting mechanics and has a high replayability. 

There are several flaws with the game design, however. First of all, the rules leave much to be desired. We found ourselves frequently referring to the strategy post in the forums (which is excellent) to decipher what some of the abilities actually meant. Further, certain aspects are never addressed, like when a card can be slayed. Several of the rules are very involved and the heavy text wears on the eyes after awhile. Finally, the initial rules are so complicated in their explanation that it takes about 20-25 minutes to teach the game.

Overall, I would recommend this game to anyone who is a fan of involved tactical card games and TCGs and has two brain cells to rub together. If you are looking for an easy game to pass time or a game that will incorporate a high strategic element (e.g. apples to apples or Axis and Allies respectively) then I would delete this game from your amazon cart promptly. 

TLDR: Fun, but complicated with a few shortcomings.

RATING: 9
Innovation is a game that really lives up to its name. It's an abstract civ-building game with a set-collection mechanic (with a nice twist - Splay that is)that takes one element of civ-building and focusses on it. Just like Roads & boats did. But where Roads & boats focussed  on logistics and thereby represented the progress of a civilization, Innovation chose an element that is just ever so logic for the flourishing of nations. Now what could be a better way to do so then by focussing on the inventions and innovations that man came up with over the ages? 

There's different innovations/inventions of all sorts. There are those that affect ecologogy, then there are those of trading, there are military inventions, there are scientifical inventions and there are cultural ideas.

You stack these cards and when the time is right (and you have the right cards) you get to splay the stack. Which means you have more icons depicted on the cards. The icons are important when it comes to the effect. So even when your opponent uses the Dogma effect of a card (which means he uses the card) you may get to benefit from it if you have enough relevant icons in play. This is a very crucial part of the game. Splaying is crucial and the direction of splay even more so. Early on in the game you get to splay your stacks to the left. Later on certain innovations will allow you to splay right or even up. These different splays reveal more icons per card:

Splay to the left:  +max. 1 icon
Splay to the right: +max. 2 icons
Splay upwards:      +max. 3 icons

Also, the type of icon might differ. So the splay kind of reminds me how you adjust your cities in Sid Meier's computer-Civilization to focus on the resource you want it to produce (knowledge, gold, food)

This game is a real brain-burner. You should take the recommendation to not play a first game with four players seriously. I have hight doubts in the stated playing time of 30 minutes. If you're familar with all the cards - and there's plenty of them and they are all different - maybe. But until now I think 30 minutes per player are more like it.

Nevertheless, the game is great fun and there's tons of meaningful decisions to take. Your opponent's plans will likely more than once make you change your strategy. Still the game is not purelxy tactical. 

8/10   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.5
The best tactical game I own.  Innovation is a game of doing the best with what you have, not planning for what you want.  If you go in expecting it to be something else, you'll find it swingy, chaotic, and disappointing.  I've seen several games in which people get discouraged and almost give up, and then some new tech shakes the world and they're back in the running. 

I actually find it mirrors the actual progression of technologies and cultures quite nicely, as in my view the really groundbreaking developments don't emerge from government think-tanks but rather from the minds of mad geniuses.  Ideas quickly spill over borders and find new, often better uses elsewhere.  So it's not who can come up with the idea to begin with -- it's who can be in the best position to adapt to and exploit a constant influx of new and powerful ideas.  Love it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
1-25-2011 -- I'm a Glory to Rome fan, so naturally, I'm really excited to play Innovation.

::UPDATE::

1/7/12 -- I've had this on my shelf for nearly a year, and I was finally able to talk my wife into playing last night. It was as great as I expected it to be. It took us a bit to become familiar with the language, but not as bad as I thought it would be. Our game was very close with wild lead swings. In the early game I felt like I was walking away with it. Then Ashley quickly came back and started snatching Achievements like it was nothing. I finally got a solid top-card empire and made some tricky offensive moves to catch up on Achievements, including the first special Acheviement. Then we were deadlocked at 5 Achievements for several turns. I was one turn away from snatching another Special Achievement when Ashley took a different one and won the game!

It was great. I think when there's time, we'll both be looking forward to another go with this one. My early impressions are all positive. I'm keeping it at a 7.5 because the randomness of the cards could potentially be a bit of a balance killer. But it didn't seem to be a problem this time around. If we play a few more and it's always a close game, I'll probably have to bump it to at least an 8.

I'm glad I own this one!

RATING: 8.5
This is one of two games this year that I think are really fun, but I'm not sure are really great (the other is Macao).  I'm not sure that the cards you receive aren't a greater influence on victory than the decisions you make.  In any case, it's very fun to play and I'm not burned out on it after 25 plays, so it has at least some staying power.

The game reminds also reminds me of the feel of another Stefan Feld game, Roma.  There can be the same landslide victories for one player - where there is no hope of recovery and the other player plows through.  But, (as in Roma) that just makes the epic back-and-forth battles that much more rewarding.  The difference between the two (besides gameplay, of course), is that getting slammed in Innovation is much more fun than in Roma.  It may last a little longer, but at least you feel like you are building something and trying to dig yourself out.

Innovation has so many different ways to win.  After several plays, I really felt a certain card was worthless - I couldn't imagine ever using it.  Sure enough, in the next game, the circumstances arose where that exact card helped me claw my way back into the game.  New combinations seem to show up frequently while learning the game.  I still feel like there's a lot to pick up after 25 plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A chaotic game that manages to be great fun despite its serious flaws.

The game's high points are infinite replayability and ease of play. It manages to be captivating, despite its randomness. The icon collection part is enjoyable, and it does give some control to an otherwise chaotic game.

Now let me clarify ease of play. This game is simple, IF you understand excellent english AND you're an experienced gamer. Better if you're a hardcore gamer. For anything from average players and below, the game is simply an unplayable chore. And no, you can't even think of playing if you have a simple "good" knowledge of the language. But even if you're eligible to play it, it is chaotic. Cards are not only random and somewhat unbalanced, but also potentially broken. Nothing is sacred, and your hard earned points can be devastated by a lucky draw. Efficient play is also surprisingly hard, and this is exactly what gives this game the impression that it is skill based. A good player will beat a bad player because the latter didn't see in time the use of his cards. But between two good, or two bad players... the result is based heavily on luck.

In the end, this game manages to be fun to an irritating(!) level. Despite its flaws, I do like it a lot, and keep asking to play it.

RATING: 10
My RftG killer. Plays best at 2. Unique card combos make every game different.

RATING: 9
Initial rating: 9 with room to go up.  I haven't been this captivated by a game in a while.  I don't just want to have it be part of the regular rotation, I want to play it over and over again.  I can really only make the same comment about Race for the Galaxy, Imperial and (back in the day) Settlers of Catan (although I've since mostly played that one out).  Two of those three are still 10s for me, so Innovation is in good company.

The mechanics are very cool, particularly the splaying of stacks of cards.  There is a lot of chaos and randomness to maneuver through, but that feels right for this type and length of game.  Many cards seem crazily powerful, but that is part of the fun rather than detracting from the experience -- especially because one game's super powerful card may have little to no impact in the following game.  What really gets me is how a game with fairly simple rules and only cards as components can give so much feel of civilization building.  The advances and their effects largely make sense:  I develop writing and speed my advance to the next age; I develop gunpowder and can now blow up your castles; etc.

2 players: 9
3 players: 7
4 players: 4

RATING: 10
Hoo Haw this is a good time.  Nice mix of screwage, development, and trade offs.  Cashing in on a good scoring opportunity comes at the cost of development, but it is tough to win without scoring opportunities.  Three games in and it seems the early game is focused around a few cards.  I love the feel of development, and the rise of a civilization.  This gives the game a nifty comeback, and a nice balance between trying the lengthen and shorten the game.  Loving this game right now.  I'm constantly uncovering new tricks and defenses.  

I'm surprised I enjoy such a tactics heavy game so much.  I guess the trade off in lack of strategy is compensated by the interesting development arc of each civilization. (Actually with more play the strategical arc has started to develop for me, its more vague in style such as ensure you aren't behind in both crowns and leaves)

There does not seem to be a normal pace in this game.  Sometimes the early is frantic, sometimes the middle, sometimes the middle and the end.  Plus sometimes points are easy to come by and easy to keep, sometimes they are impossible to get and tougher to keep.  This game does not seem to have a normal type game.  Its a game of constantly shifting pacing and style, with very situational plays.  This is why I like it a lot, and really enjoy it.  I think its a 2 player game for my liking, but time will tell.

7 plays in.  Easy 10.  I have fun playing it (I play by the rules) and the game forces you to learn and understand the possible ways to get ahead.  I'm awful at this game and keep coming back for more.

I enjoy the decisions present, the trade offs, and playing the hand I'm dealt.  The thing that sets this game apart in my eyes is my anticipation and curiosity of which card will be the superstar this game, based upon game states.

This game will leave you frustrated from time to time.  Not in the Agricola sense of "There are so many things I want to do, but I don't have the time" but more in general helplessness due to the weakness of the  basic action compared to dogmas in the right situation.   

At the time of writing this comment, I have played this game at least once for 8 straight days and I'm still enthralled.  Well it was a 10 before me and my opponent became better, and well quite frankly it has even become more enjoyable to play since we improved our skill.  This game will be a staple for years to come for me.

After 42 plays, I have started to see the game in a slightly different light strategy wise.  The interplay between icons, future innovations based upon those icons, short term scoring vs. long term development and the end game.  This is deeper than I first thought, rating going up...... oh shit its already a 10.  Ummm... Its a really really good 10.

It is significantly better at lower player counts.  I'd grab another game if 4 players are looking to play something (haven't tried the team rules)

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
There's a core idea here that I really liked, which seemed quite true to the supposed theme of the advancing science and technology of civilizations. Basically you can only have five cards in play at one time (one of each color). Cards have a several icons on them which count towards a handful of metrics. At first there are a lot of "castles," but these disappear and give way to things like "factories" and then "timers." The ability of the card is also tied to a single metric. If the ability hurts another player, it only works against someone inferior in that metric. If the ability helps yourself, everyone superior in that metric gets to piggyback off of your move and also use it before you. So there can be tension between the need to improve what you have in play (generally you'd like to have cards from the latest eras possible; or at least cards with good abilities), and the "arms race" of a given metric, since "upgrading" a card in play could hurt in you a metric that currently matters.

Overall though it loses points for all the reading and re-reading of cards, and for special abilities in general (not my cup of tea), and for the luck of the draw being so important. It seems to happen very frequently that despite having a bunch of cards in hand and in play, nothing will do what you need, so you just draw a new card and hope it gets you somewhere. You can also e.g. luck into a card that lets you drain someone else's points turn after turn.

A play of the game may tell an interesting story but there's little potential for negotiation. Offensive powers always target everyone they can, and nothing can be traded.

...Playing again with different expectations. I can certainly see this as a civilization game in the way the advancement of technologies defines a course for the game, with varying gameplay over that course. The symbols and especially colors are more abstract than expected for the genre, but there's no mistaking that the colored piles in your tableau constitute your civilization.

While I don't normally like special powers, in this game there is nothing other than special powers. I can respect it as a fairly simple system (as I like simple systems) that happens to be based on special powers.

I criticize the game as random and chaotic, but it's also tactical, and making the best move at a given moment is not necessarily easy, not necessarily pointless (i.e. drowned out by luck). I can respect the puzzles that Innovation presents. I have no doubt that one can be more skilled or less skilled at playing.

There are many cards which affect other players, making it a very interactive game.

RATING: 6
Chaotic, but not so much so that you can't form at least some kind of plan. That's probably also highly dependent on how much you're able to commit to learning the game deeply. I'm not.

The theme didn't really come through for me. I very quickly stopped even reading the names of most of the cards since they seemed to have so little to do with what the card did.

Some of the terminology is stupid. You "meld" a card ... even if there's no other card yet for it to meld with.  Why not just "play" the card, like in every other game on the planet? And "dogma" is a complete non-sequitur as a description for activating most cards' abilities (e.g. what the heck is "gunpowder dogma?"). Could it at least have used a *verb*, like with the other actions? This stuff makes the game needlessly harder to learn.

Ultimately, though, none of this ruins the game for me. It's pretty pleasant, and I would happily play again. And, of course, the card-splaying design is quite clever, so points for that.

インスト可
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This isn't a game about calculating the optimal play & then repeat. You don't build an engine because anything you build is transient. This tactical transiency feeds constant drama, a state that persists in this game from beginning to end, and after over a hundred plays it maintains that interest & drama. 

You learn it as a purely tactical game, but as you get familiar with the deck (not memorize, just familiar) strategy rises to structure the tactical riches.

During a game of Innovation an amazing array of choices rise - and I don't mean the same choices arise game after game so you get to script a good combo tactic - I mean this stuff [i]does not repeat[/i]. Each card is unique, yes, but the context of that card (it's relationship to each other card currently in play, to the game state, and to the opponent's style of play) radically changes not only it's usefulness, but what it can do, whether or not actions could be leeched, and what it means. And as your mind ratchets through the possibilities it zero's in on a choice but you never really know it's [i]the most optimal[/i]. It's a playground. 

And also, in Innovation there is constant direct interaction with the other player & with their cards; this game is about screwage, not simply leeching. And man can it rebound on you.

Easily one of my very favorite games. It's witty as well - each game forms a narrative, and there's wit in the matching of card names to effects.

I've gone from not understanding this game, to finally being to get a feel for what's going on. That's after 30 games. 

The first few ages are quite familiar, but the final ages are incredibly perplexing. The higher ages start snowballing to the endgame in an out of control fashion. Fantastic game!

Wow, I've been getting lots of plays against the AI and finally beat the hardest level AI (100%). Won by trying to draw an age 11 card with 150 points in the bank to 0. Mind you, it's not all about the points!

You lose the ability to respond quickly enough to the changing landscape due to another person taking the potential cards that could help alter the course of the game. If you fall behind as well, you're really stuck. I also don't think the expansions are required at all. 90%+ of the games I've played have been base game only and it still has legs.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I like the mechanics more than the resultant game.

1 game to learn how to play- TONS more games before you are playing with enough knowledge of the cards and potential paths that your informed decisions come close to overpowering the random elements inherent in the system.

which is to say, for those tons of learning games, you're mostly making purely tactical decisions and hoping cards come up in your favor and not in your opponent's.  the fluxx comparison isn't wholly accurate, as EVENTUALLY you will be well enough apprised of the situation that your choices are meaningful (whereas you can never really reach that point in fluxx).  

the real issue for me is that, while i'm ok with a steep learning curve,  i kind of want the interaction during that curve to still be interesting, and it's not-  neither while i'm learning, nor while i would be playing with any new player trying to catch up on the curve...  because until you crest that peak, you are pretty much just in random land.  and that's a lot of bad games until you reach the good stuff... and then some more bad games whenever you want a new, real opponent.  and once everyone IS at a similar skill level, then the random elements are generally what's determining the winner, not clever play.  There's a very small window of "interesting and competitive play".

I'm cool with a steep learning curve, but I'd prefer it not be a curve that is mandatory, long, and unfun... but necessary to get over the hump of the random elements in the game.

RATING: 8
Amazing 2p game, haven't seen anything like it. Everything about this game is unusual: the cards (every card is unique! what? seriously.), the effects, the terminology (ever melded/splayed cards before?), the scoring, the unexpected (but thematic) endgame conditions

Note: there's a strong learning curve with this one; don't expect any startegy nor thematic flavor in the first few games. once you start knowing a few cards, you will start seeing the strategies and the theme shining through; then you'll know more cards, and more strategies, and it grows thematically strong in the process; and for me it keeps improving after many plays!

[+] rules are easy, cards and art are nice, outisde-the-box-thinking is required, gameplay improves and refines with experience 
[-] although the box say it's a 2-4p game, it really shines with 2. 3 kinda works... 4 with teams is fiddly... 4p is a mess.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After one play, I'm putting this at an 8. I feel it could move to a 9 if it remains well received after more plays. 

This felt like Through The Ages (A game I've only had a chance to play on Boardgamearena.com) but in a more digestible format. 

It has the abstract concepts turned into game mechanics (Tech tree)that is one of my biggest draws to civ building games, and quite a bit of player interaction.

 There are multiple ways to win, and they feel interlinked and yet varied enough to be balanced. My favorite mechanic was that the "score" in the game is what determines winner if the game reaches it's "out of cards" game end, BUT they score is always used throughout the game to pay purchase game ending (when you get 6) achievement cards as well. 

Overall, the game has that slow start, momentum gaining feel that a civ game should have, leading into a headlong tech race once you advance a few ages. One complaint is that there is no unique civ/player powers, but the gameplay actually leads you to develop these through the cards you play anyway so it's not really a true negative. 

My biggest criticism was that I lost to a card-specific victory condition that was played and triggered on one turn, meaning I had no way to try to counter it. Of course, I could have been attempting to get my opponent to discard cards, or randomly deny them resources, but I had no warning of what he would need to beat me with that card beforehand. This will likely be less of an issue if you play the same game a lot since you will know about when that card could come up.

Anyway, this game is great in a small package. The rules are a bit dense, but it's nothing that 15-20 mins of teaching and Q&A won't handle.

RATING: 4
I've given this game two tries, and I think that's enough to determine that this is simply not my kind of game.  I'm puzzled by the strong reputation this game has, because all it seems to be to me is a big game of take that, with players hitting other players for points and such.  There's some semblance of control in the game, since you can protect yourself with the number of icons you have in your display, but you're still more or less at the mercy of the draw, and besides, the game then becomes a very random, chaotic game of area control.  I don't want to have to play something like that.  It's also nearly impossible to plan ahead in this game, since the entire state of the game can change before your next turn, either through changing icons or someone swapping hands of cards with you.  Either way, I'm not interested.

One thing I thought was interesting about the game was that it appears to be easy for people to lose sight of the achievements, at the cost of building up a boatload of points each turn.  In one of the games I played, one player probably could have ended the game at the halfway mark by taking achievements, but he got blinded by the pursuit of more points.  It reminded me of early games of Dominion, where players got distracted by the action cards.

2 plays: 4 players

RATING: N/A
This new game by Carl Chudyk is a journey through innovations from the stone age through modern times. Each player builds a civilization based on various technologies, ideas, and cultural advancements, all represented by cards. Each of these cards has a unique power which will allow further advancement, point scoring, or even attacking other civilizations. Be careful though, as other civilizations may be able to benefit from your ideas as well!
\13\\13\
To win, you must score achievements, which you can attain by amassing points or by meeting certain criteria with the innovations you have built. Plan your civilization well, and outmaneuver your opponents, and with some luck you will achieve victory!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A civilization game that has been abstracted and condensed down until it's simulating nothing more than the race through mankind's technology tree. The game is pure genius, but maybe to its detriment, because its design is so far outside the box that it can be hard to wrap your head around it...at least strategically.

Your first few games, it can be difficult to understand where you have any control of your strategy. It will seem very random. Fluxx-like, as the game's detractors say. Draw a card, play a card, or activate a card...choose two each turn. That's it. 

Innovation does so much with so little rules overhead that its considerable depth is often missed. There's no military to speak of, but there certainly is war. There's no economy to run, but you can bankrupt yourself out of being able to function. The fact that the game creates this dynamic with such a basic turn structure is absolutely amazing.

This has become the 2-player card game of choice in my house and I really don't see that changing any time soon. Ten games in and I'm still finding new ways to move faster up the tech tree or to throw monkey wrenches into my opponent's plans. Don't believe the one game and done critics, this game gets deeper and more complex with every single play.
